1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2

5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""

reasoning
I'm breaking down the architecture of this model with four blocks—three KDA blocks and one MLA block—each containing attention plus a mixture-of-experts FFN layer with normalization and residual connections. The hidden dimension is 2304, and for the KDA blocks with 32 heads and 128 dimensions per head, the projections scale up to 4096, with various quantization and precision choices for different components. For the MLA blocks, the projection dimensions are different—the query projects to 6144 while the key-value components use smaller intermediate dimensions. The MoE layer routes to 64 experts with a top-8 selection plus one shared expert, and I'm calculating the weight sizes in bytes for each component, accounting for int4 quantization and the scale/zero parameters. The MLA's key-value projection is particularly interesting because it applies across the entire cache during decoding, which creates significant compute, but there's an optimization trick where this computation gets absorbed into the decode process. The key insight is that instead of computing the full key-value projection for every position in the cache, I can rearrange the matrix multiplication to compute an effective query first, then do a single matrix-vector product with the cache. This transforms the attention computation over the cache into just a GEMV operation with the cache dimensions and the number of heads. After computing attention scores with the rope component and applying softmax, I accumulate the weighted sum of cache values across all positions, then project through the output weight matrix per head. The bottleneck is the cache reads—at 16K positions, that's about 16.8MB of data that needs to be read twice (once for scores, once for the weighted sum), though I could potentially fuse this into a single pass using online softmax to accumulate the weighted sum while computing scores. For the MoE layer, I'm routing tokens to 8 active experts plus 1 shared expert, each with gate, up, and down projections. The weight matrices total around 31.9MB per MoE block when quantized to int4, with scales and zeros adding minimal overhead. Across 4 blocks, that's roughly 127MB of MoE parameters to load per token. Putting it together: the attention cache reads (~17MB), embedding lookups (~23.6MB), and MoE weights (~127MB) sum to around 213MB of data movement per token. At the memory bandwidth ceiling of 1.8TB/s, that's a theoretical floor of about 120 microseconds. The baseline approach materializes everything in bf16, which multiplies the traffic by roughly 9× and spawns far more kernel launches, making it significantly slower. The real challenge is executing the entire forward pass in a single kernel launch to avoid overhead. I'm considering two paths: a cooperative CUDA kernel that uses grid-wide synchronization across all SMs to coordinate the four sequential blocks, or a Triton kernel with software barriers built from atomics to achieve the same effect. CUDA cooperative groups is the cleaner approach since it has explicit grid synchronization primitives. I'm planning to write a raw CUDA megakernel that launches enough blocks to keep all SMs resident, then uses cooperative synchronization between each stage of the forward pass. For the actual computation, if we're using KDA, each token goes through RMSNorm first, then splits into several projection paths: queries, keys, and values get projected to 4096 dimensions via int4 quantized GEMVs, while a gating signal and beta parameter get computed separately. The convolution step then applies a sliding window over the previous context, combining the current q/k/v with stored history through a series of taps, applying SiLU activation, and shifting the window state forward. After that, the gating signal modulates the attention scores exponentially. Appending the cached kv and k_rope to memory, then handling absorbed attention by computing effective queries through matrix multiplication with the kv_b weights, which are int4 quantized and organized so each head's 256-column block maps to both the nope dimensions and value dimensions. continuing through the attention computation—for each head I'm computing attention scores by combining the effective query with cached key-value pairs, then running online softmax to accumulate the context. After flattening the per-head outputs, I apply the output projection and add it back to the residual. Then I normalize again and compute router logits for the mixture-of-experts layer. For the MoE routing, I'm selecting the top 8 experts via softmax, normalizing their weights, and for each routed expert computing the gated linear unit activation before projecting through the expert network—accumulating weighted contributions from all 8 plus a shared expert. The entire computation is memory-bound with int4 quantization and fused dequantization, and the megakernel uses cooperative blocks with grid synchronization between dependent stages. Now I'm mapping out the pipeline stages: starting with layer norm on the input, then the QKV+gate GEMV, followed by convolution and state update to produce the output vector, then residual connection, another norm, router computation with top-k selection, expert GEMVs, output projection, and final residual. The key constraint is identifying where grid synchronization is actually needed—some operations can be fused together if they don't have cross-block dependencies, but the layer norm requires a sync since all blocks need the normalized input before proceeding. The state tensor S is stored as fp32 with shape [H, Dk, Dv], so each head reads and updates its 128×128 block independently within a single block. Computing the prediction involves a reduction over the Dk dimension (S transposed times k), and the output similarly reduces S times q—both operations fit within one block per head. The reference implementation keeps state in fp32 throughout, so I need to maintain that precision rather than converting. For the convolution windows, the query/key/value pre-conv and previous window data (3×4096 each) are stored as bf16 in the state, with updates handled via shifting. After the output projection (4096→2304 GEMV plus residual), I sync. Then in the expert routing stage, one block computes the router GEMV (2304×64 bf16) and top-8 selection, writing the indices and weights to workspace so all blocks can access them for their expert GEMVs—requiring another sync point after routing. The challenge is that the router depends on the normalized residual output, creating a dependency chain: attention output → residual → normalization → router → top-k → expert GEMVs. Computing the router redundantly across all 188 blocks would cost 55MB of bandwidth, which is too expensive. I'm weighing whether to compute it in stage D alongside the output projection, or keep it separate in stage E with one block handling it while others wait, versus using atomics to coordinate without explicit syncs. So the key insight is that while stage E computes the router and topk (cheap operations), the other blocks can parallelize by computing the shared expert's gate and up projections—they need the normalized residual anyway, so that redundant computation is acceptable given the traffic is comparable. Then in stage F, we handle the routed experts' gate and up operations plus the shared expert's down projection, which now has its intermediate results ready from the previous stage. For stage G, I'm working through how to accumulate the routed expert outputs back into the main residual. Each routed expert produces a 2304-dimensional output weighted by its gating score, and we need to sum these across all 8 experts. The challenge is coordinating the writes—either use atomic adds directly into the output accumulator (straightforward but potentially contended), or have each block own a slice of the output columns and iterate through all experts' contributions to that slice, which trades atomics for more memory reads of the down weight matrices. For the down GEMV itself, the weight matrices are stored per-expert with shape (512, 2304) in row-major format, and I'm parallelizing over output columns. Each block handles a column range and loops through the top-k experts, reading the corresponding weight stripe. The key detail is that with column chunks of 128+ elements, the memory access pattern becomes coalesced since we're reading contiguous bytes per row, so the strided access across rows stays efficient. Each thread loads 4 columns per row with coalesced memory access, then accumulates sums in registers while broadcasting x values from shared memory or L1 cache. For the full problem with 4 matrices and thousands of columns, I'm looking at how to distribute work across many blocks and threads to saturate the GPU — roughly 48K threads across 188 SMs to handle the GEMV operations efficiently. The challenge is that with 4096 threads each processing 4 columns sequentially through 1152 rows, we risk becoming latency-bound with insufficient outstanding memory requests. A split-K approach where each block handles both a column tile and a K-slice, accumulating partial results in shared memory or registers then using atomic operations to combine them into a floating-point workspace, would help hide latency and improve occupancy. For precision, the reference implementation uses fp32 throughout while the baseline uses bf16, with a loose tolerance of 0.98 cosine similarity. I can accumulate in fp32 and keep intermediates in fp32, then round to bf16 when storing to the convolution windows to match the reference behavior where q_proj outputs are stored as bf16. The state updates that check.py validates are S and c_kv, not the conv windows directly, though the windows do affect future steps. Now I'm working through the split-K atomic approach, which requires zeroing workspaces between stages. I could zero small fp32 buffers cheaply within the kernel or use a separate accumulate-then-reduce pattern. Alternatively, I could avoid split-K entirely by distributing work per-thread to get enough parallelism. Looking at the GEMV cost: the 4.7MB matrix at 1.8TB/s takes about 2.6µs, and with 188 SMs and 4096 columns by 2304 K dimension, I need to figure out how many threads per block can handle the work efficiently. I'm exploring a tiling strategy that handles both column and K dimensions: a block processes a 64-column tile across all K, with 256 threads split as 16 threads across columns and 16 K-partitions, so each K-partition covers 72 packed rows. The reduction happens across K-partitions via shared memory, and the grid would be 256 blocks for stage B, which looks reasonable. Each block reads about 73KB of data. Now I'm checking memory coalescing: a warp of 32 threads reading 64 bytes contiguously per row-load should map cleanly to modern GPU sector-based L2 caching, so there's no wasted bandwidth. I'm considering simplifying further with a 128-column tile handled by 32 threads instead. For the full 4096-column matrix, I'd need 32 blocks in stage B, giving 128 total blocks across the pipeline—slightly underutilizing the 188 SMs available, so I could either shrink to 64-column tiles for 256 blocks or expand to 512 threads per block with 128-column tiles to improve occupancy. Rather than over-optimize now, I'll sketch out a persistent megakernel design: a single kernel launched cooperatively across G blocks (likely 2–4 blocks per SM given resource constraints, so G could reach ~564), where each stage distributes work via a loop over task IDs with grid stride, then synchronizes across the grid between stages—this keeps the design flexible regardless of actual grid size. I need to be careful about when to capture pointers — they're only valid after the model is moved to device and weights are loaded, so I'll build the pointer table lazily on the first step() call. For the MLA cache, the reference implementation allocates new tensors each step via torch.cat, which means significant memory traffic (around 16.8MB per step for the cache concatenation), but that's unavoidable if we need to return updated state with appended rows. I could preallocate a larger buffer upfront and return narrowed views instead of copying the whole cache each time — this would amortize the copy cost across multiple steps since the benchmark runs 16-32 sequential steps per trial. The correctness check compares shapes and cosine similarity, so a view of a larger buffer works fine. The question is whether I can avoid even the initial copy when I get the state tensor and need to write a new row. The constraint is that the decode step must be a single GPU kernel launch, so any cache import or copy has to happen inside the megakernel itself. I could keep an internal preallocated buffer with capacity, and on the first step (or if the incoming state tensors aren't already my buffer views), I'd need to copy them in — but that copy would have to be part of the kernel work, with some thread blocks handling the L×512 element transfer as part of the overall computation. The bandwidth cost is manageable: ~16.8MB per trajectory import, which amortizes to about 1MB per step over 16 steps, and since MLA is layer 3, that import can overlap with layers 0-2 anyway. The simpler approach is to have the megakernel write new rows into my capacity buffer while attention reads from the original incoming tensor on the first step, then on subsequent steps the buffer identity check passes (since step returns a view of my buffer) and no copy is needed. For validation, check.py compares cosine similarity across the whole cache—since my copied cache is bit-identical to the original L rows plus the new row, the cosine check trivially passes even if the new row is garbage, though correct attention still requires the new row to be right. The Python-side per-step logic just creates metadata views and does CPU-side bookkeeping via data_ptr checks, which is fine, but I need to be careful about the benchmark loop reusing returned state since each step appends and grows the view—I'm allocating capacity for L plus 40 rows to handle up to 32 steps plus warmup. Each benchmark._run call starts fresh with a new init_state and external tensor, so my model must detect when the cache is new via data_ptr mismatch and re-import it. The timed loop runs up to 32 steps, and I'm using a capacity margin of 64 to be safe. The check.py test runs a single step per state, and the solution handles contexts of 2048, 8192, and 16384. One thing to watch: torch.empty for the capacity buffer is a CPU-side cudaMalloc, not a kernel launch, so that should be allowed. To handle this cleanly, I'll allocate the capacity buffer lazily only when the incoming cache isn't ours—that happens on the first step of each _run, which is still timed but the allocation overhead is small thanks to the caching allocator. Rather than preallocate a massive buffer at model init (which wouldn't know the context length anyway), I'll allocate on demand with a generous capacity of L + 96 and keep it cached per context. For subsequent steps, I can use an identity check: compare the data pointer and shape to verify it's our buffer, or more reliably, check if the returned state dict contains the same view object using `is`. Since the reference implementation mutates the state dict in place and we return the same dict object, the identity check should work across calls. The edge case with KDA's "S" state is that the reference replaces it with a new tensor each step, so I need to handle that functional update pattern. In the benchmark scenario where state is reused across steps, in-place updates are fine as long as I'm careful about the order of operations. The key constraint is that I can't modify the input before using it, and when updating S in place within the kernel, I need to ensure each head block reads its portion of S fully into registers or shared memory before writing back, avoiding race conditions from grid-stride assignments that might split a head across multiple blocks. Now I'm working through the memory requirements for staging S during computation. The RTX PRO 6000 has 128KB of shared memory per SM with up to 99KB available per block, which is tight for a 128×128 fp32 matrix. But looking at the actual computation flow, I need two passes over S anyway: first to compute the prediction by reducing over the dk dimension with decay applied, then to update S and compute the output. This means I could either stage S once and reuse it, or read it twice from global memory depending on what's more efficient. The key insight is that S is only 2MB total across all 32 heads per layer, and with two reads plus one write that's 6MB of traffic. Since the L2 cache on GB202 is around 96-128MB, both passes will hit L2 on the second read, so the overhead of reading from global twice is minimal. The simplest approach is to just do two passes from global memory and let the cache handle it, rather than trying to squeeze everything into shared memory. For the MLA attention with online softmax, I'm looking at how to parallelize across the sequence length of up to 16K tokens. The challenge is that online softmax requires cross-block reduction to combine partial results from different chunks of the sequence. The strategy is a two-phase approach: each block computes partial statistics (max and sum) for its chunk of the sequence for one head, storing 512 floats of accumulation per block-head pair. With 16K tokens split into 1024-token chunks, that's 16 chunks times 32 heads equals 512 partial results totaling 1MB, which is manageable. Then a reduction stage combines these partials with rescaling—essentially a split-KV flash decode pattern with two stages and synchronization between them. Now I'm reconsidering the memory layout: the cache is shared across all heads, so I can't have each head-block read the entire cache independently—that would blow up to 537MB. Instead, I need to tile over the sequence length with all heads processed together in a single block. Each block handles a 1024-token chunk for all 32 heads, reading just 1MB of cache. For each token in the chunk, I compute the attention score across all 32 heads using the effective query, which is 64KB of fp32 data that fits in L2. The computation is a matrix multiply of 1024 tokens by 512 dimensions by 32 heads, giving about 16 million operations per block, and with 16 blocks that's 268 million FLOPs total—well within budget. But the issue is that this GEMM at 1024×512 with 32 heads only gives 33 MACs per byte of memory, which isn't great utilization. With only 188 SMs and just 16 blocks active, I'm leaving a lot of hardware idle. I should shrink the chunks to 128 rows instead, which means 128 blocks running in parallel. Each block reads 128KB of cache and does 2 million MACs, plus it accumulates partial results for all 32 heads—that's 64KB of fp32 partials per block. Across 128 blocks, I get 8MB of partial results that need to be reduced, which involves reading those 8MB back and summing the rescaled partials for each head. Trying a 256-row chunk instead: 64 blocks with 4MB of partials, plus the 16.8MB context cache and 4-8MB of extra partial traffic—that's workable. I could also split by head-group and L-chunk, but that balloons the cache reads to 67MB, which is worse. So I'll stick with the partial-based approach using 256-row chunks. For different context lengths, this scales nicely: 16K context gives 64 blocks, 8K gives 32, 2K gives 8 (underutilized but still fast in absolute time). Each block loads the query embeddings, a chunk of key-value cache, and the rope embeddings for both. Now I'm computing attention scores per head and tracking the max and sum of exponentials, accumulating the weighted values. The inner loop does about 4.2M multiply-accumulates per block across 256 threads, which should be around 4-5 microseconds. But that's potentially compute-bound with fp32 CUDA cores—the memory bandwidth for 256KB is only 0.14 microseconds, so I might be spending more time on arithmetic than loading data. For the full 16K context, the total is around 537M multiply-accumulates in fp32, which is a lot. On Blackwell's 110 TFLOPs fp32, that's roughly 10 microseconds—acceptable within the 150 microsecond budget, but I should consider using bf16 packed math or tensor cores to speed it up. I could load the key-value data as bf16 pairs, convert to float2, and use fused multiply-add, though the conversion overhead might eat into gains. For now, 10 microseconds is fine for a first version; I can optimize later with tensor core operations like mma.m16n8k16 for the score computation if needed. For the score GEMM itself, I could hand-roll a small matrix multiply using tensor cores to cut it down to around 1 microsecond. Now I'm thinking through the synchronization stages per block type—the KDA block needs to fuse the GEMV operations for query, key, value, and gate projections, plus the beta computation in bf16. The tricky part is that each block redundantly computes the RMSNorm of the input, which requires reading the input and the normalization weights, then doing a reduction for the reciprocal square root. For the rmsnorm computation, I can fold it into the GEMV blocks themselves—each block redundantly computes the inverse norm over the 2304 elements and applies it on the fly when loading x into shared memory, which is faster than coordinating across blocks. The same approach works for moe_norm. Then for the output projection stage, the GEMV blocks writing to 2304 columns can directly add the residual if each column stays within a single block, avoiding split-K atomics—with 18 blocks handling the columns, it's underutilized but clean. For the residual accumulation, I'm using atomicAdd into a pre-initialized buffer that stage C fills during its idle time (blocks 32 and beyond copy x into the accumulator), then stage D's GEMV partials atomically add into it, giving us the updated x after synchronization. The router and topk logic runs in a single block, but the next layer's attention needs the updated x plus the MoE output, which isn't available yet, so there's a sync point before moving to the routed gate operations. For the routed down path, I'm pre-seeding the x2 accumulator in stage E using idle blocks, then the routed down GEMV needs the computed hh values (silu(gate) * up). The gate and up GEMVs produce their outputs via atomics or single-owner writes, and rather than precomputing hh into shared memory, the down GEMV can compute hh on the fly as it reads each element — multiplying silu(g[m]) * u[m] for each row of the 1024-element kernel. For the overall pipeline, I'm balancing where to place the shared down operation: it could go in stage F after the shared gate/up are ready, or in stage G with the routed down. Putting shared down in F gives roughly 3.6MB of work there, while G handles the 8 routed down GEMVs at 9.4MB. Either way, the next block's stage B needs the completed acc3, so I'm syncing after each stage boundary — that's 5 syncs total per KDA block (after B, C, D, E, F, and G). For the MLA block, the pipeline is more complex: stage B' does the q_proj and kv_a GEMVs, then C1 handles rope, append, and the q_eff computation (which involves transposed GEMVs across 32 head-blocks), C2 runs attention partials over L-chunks, C3 combines those partials and applies W_v^T to get the output, and D finishes with o_proj and residual. Adding the MoE stages E, F, G on top of that brings the total to around 8 syncs for the MLA block. So across the full step I'm looking at roughly 26-30 grid.sync calls total. The latency concern is real—if each grid.sync costs 2-5µs, that's potentially 45-150µs of overhead, which eats significantly into the ~120µs budget. Modern GPUs can do cooperative grid.sync in around 1-2µs, so 30 syncs at 1.5µs each gives about 45µs, which is tight but maybe workable. Still, I should explore ways to reduce the sync count—either by merging stages more aggressively or replacing some grid.sync calls with flag-based producer-consumer synchronization, though that adds complexity. The KDA pipeline could be compressed from B→C→D→E→F→G down to fewer stages, and the MLA block could potentially fold some of its stages together. The challenge is that merging D and E doesn't work cleanly because E needs the x2 output from D to be complete before it can proceed. Merging F into E is also problematic since F's routed GEMVs depend on the topk values computed in E. I could compute the router redundantly across every block to avoid the sync, but that's expensive—the router GEMV is 2304×64 in bf16, which is 300KB, and having 250 blocks each recompute it would generate 75MB of L2 traffic, adding roughly 10µs of overhead. That's not a win. The real bottleneck is the sync itself. One option is to use fewer, larger blocks—maybe 188 or 132 blocks instead of 250—since grid.sync cost scales somewhat with block count. Looking at actual sync latencies on A100/H100, grid.sync runs around 1-3µs, so 26 syncs would be roughly 52µs total. That's meaningful but probably acceptable for a first version—the baseline is likely 2-5ms per token when materializing all weights and running dequant kernels plus matmuls, so even 300µs of overhead would be a 10× speedup. I can optimize the sync count later through techniques like flag-based syncs. A better approach might be to reduce the number of syncs altogether by restructuring how blocks coordinate. Instead of full grid syncs, I could use lightweight spin-waiting on atomic counters for producer-consumer synchronization between dependent stages, letting blocks proceed at different times and overlap independent work like cache imports or weight prefetching. With all blocks resident from cooperative launches, spinning is safe—producers atomically increment a counter when done, and consumers spin until it reaches the expected value. This enables pipelining across stages. But grid.sync is simpler and more robust, so I'll start there, measure the actual overhead, and optimize only if needed. Now I'm deciding between Triton and CUDA: grid.sync requires CUDA cooperative groups, which Triton can't do directly (atomics-based barriers are possible but messier). I'm going with CUDA C++ and load_inline, targeting sm_120 architecture. I need to use cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel to ensure all blocks are resident, and I'll query occupancy to set the grid size appropriately. For the precision path, I'm comparing against an fp32 reference where QuantLinear does fp32 matrix-vector multiply with fp32 dequantized weights. My fused GEMV kernel needs to dequantize each element on the fly—converting scales and zeros from bf16 to float, nibble weights to float, and keeping activations in fp32 for the FMA operations to hit that ≥0.98 cosine similarity target. I can optimize this by refactoring per quantization group: instead of dequantizing and multiplying each element separately, I precompute the group sum of activations once per block (reused across all output columns), then for each column just compute the weighted dot product within that group and scale it. This saves redundant operations since the activation sum is shared. The actual dot product uses fp32 FMA since activations stay in fp/bf16 precision—integer operations like dp4a wouldn't help here. For the compute intensity, a stage like 2304×4096 is about 38M MACs, which at ~100 TFLOP is negligible, so this is definitely memory-bound. The real bottleneck is saturating DRAM bandwidth with int4 quantized weights. I'm exploring vectorized uint4 loads (16 bytes per load = 32 packed values) with a row-major layout for the quantized weights, where each thread loads 16B chunks along consecutive columns. Different thread-to-column mappings trade off occupancy: assigning 8 columns per thread (4B uint2) gives a warp 256 columns and a block 256 columns across 8 warps, which yields only 16 blocks per matrix—too few for good occupancy. Tighter packing with 4 columns per thread might help, but I'm still working through the occupancy-bandwidth tradeoff. column stride is 4KB apart, so each 4B load hits a separate 32B sector—that's only 12.5% cache efficiency. But looking at the warp-level coalescing: 32 threads loading 4 bytes each from consecutive columns gives 128 contiguous bytes per instruction, which perfectly fills 4 sectors. The per-thread row accesses are strided, but the warp as a whole maintains good coalescing. Unrolling 8 rows keeps 1MB in flight across 1024 warps, which is plenty. For the quantization parameters, each warp reads 256B per group-row across the scales and zeros tensors. The x vector gets preloaded into shared memory with fused RMSNorm—I need to compute the inverse RMS via block reduction before the actual computation starts. Each block loads 2304 floats and runs the reduction, which takes well under a microsecond across the 256 threads, and with ~128 blocks overlapping anyway, the overhead is negligible. For precision, I'm keeping the normalized values in fp32 rather than bf16 to avoid rounding errors, even though the reference uses bf16—the extra accuracy is worth it and doesn't hurt performance. Now moving into the KDA gemv stage: the beta computation is a tiny bf16 linear layer (2304×32) that one block handles, producing q, k, v, and g in fp32 workspace. For each of the 32 head-blocks in stage C, I'm applying a convolution over the query using a sliding window of the last three states plus the new query slice, with learned tap weights per channel, then applying silu activation—and I need to shift the state buffer in-place to keep the window updated for the next iteration. The gate computation applies negative softplus to get the decay factor, and then I'm reading the state matrix S[h] to perform the state update math in fp32. For the shared memory layout, I'm deciding between staging the full 128×128 state matrix in SMEM (which needs about 64KB plus overhead) versus using a two-pass approach through L2 cache. The SMEM approach constrains occupancy to 1 block per SM with 80KB allocated, which limits the grid to 188 blocks—but that's exactly our design target anyway. The L2 alternative avoids SMEM pressure by reading the 2MB state twice at L2 bandwidth, adding minimal latency. I'm now settling on the two-pass within-block strategy: first pass reads the state and computes partial sums for the prediction vector (a column reduction over the decay-scaled rows), second pass re-reads from L1 cache (which stays hot) to update values. This keeps SMEM usage minimal—just enough for thread-local reductions—and lets me define the memory layout clearly: state is row-major with rows indexed by the key dimension and columns by the value dimension, with each thread handling a subset of elements. For the output accumulation, I'm reducing across columns using the same two-partials-per-column scheme, then writing the result to a workspace buffer as bf16 while keeping the fp32 computation internally. The o_proj GEMV will read from this workspace, converting back as needed. Meanwhile, the idle blocks in stage C initialize accumulators for stage D's residual computation and zero out workspaces for the next iteration. Stage D then runs the o_proj GEMV to project from 4096 dimensions down to 2304. Now I'm working through the block tiling strategy for this GEMV — with 2048 packed rows and a column tile of 128, I get 18 blocks, which is well under the 188-block limit. I can split the K dimension into quarters to get 72 total blocks, using atomic additions to accumulate the fp32 output into the x-accumulator that was seeded with the input x. The exact tiling parameters (column tile size, K-split factor) need tuning, but the core idea is to have each thread handle multiple columns efficiently. For the router stage, I'm computing the RMSnorm of x, then multiplying by the weight matrix to get logits, applying softmax, selecting the top 8 experts, and normalizing their weights by 2.446 before writing the indices and weights to workspace. The shared expert blocks then run their own GEMVs to expand from 2304 dimensions to 1024. I realize I can optimize the accumulation buffer: instead of seeding a separate accumulator for the MoE output, I can reuse the persistent fp32 buffer that already holds x2 from stage D. The MoE down GEMVs atomically add directly into this buffer to produce x3, eliminating the need for initialization since the buffer already contains the right starting value. For precision, using fp32 to accumulate residuals across blocks is close enough to the reference's bf16 approach—the difference falls within bf16 rounding error anyway. Now for stage F, I'm setting up the routed expert computation with 8 experts, each having 2 weight matrices for the gate/up projection (2304→1024), where expert indices come from the topk selection and blocks read these indices from the workspace to fetch the right weight pointers. Each expert's output goes into separate fp32 accumulators for gate and up (single-owner per column, so no atomics needed), then the down projection computes silu(gate)*up on the fly by loading the gate and up slices from shared memory and fusing the activation with the multiplication before writing the result back to the hidden buffer. Stage G handles the final routed down projection across all 8 experts. For the cache import, I'm weighing whether to batch it into the early stages (B' and C1) where it'd dominate the timeline, or spread it across all of layers 0–2 as extra grid-stride tasks to amortize the cost. The import is necessary only if flagged at launch, and at 16.8MB it's quick (~10µs), but it needs to finish before attention stage C2. Simplest approach: handle it in the very first stage of the step. The amortized cost works out to negligible—even if the import adds 10µs on the first step of a run, spread across 16 steps that's only ~0.6µs per step on average. I also need to handle the k_rope cache the same way (2.1MB at 16K context length). For stage C1, I'm computing rope embeddings on-the-fly for both q_rope and k_rope using the current position and precomputed frequency values. Computing per-row sums through thread-level accumulation and warp reduction, where each thread processes 4 columns across 2 rows (unpacking from the byte representation), then reduces across the warp to finalize the q_eff outputs. Now refactoring the dequantization formula by precomputing group-level constants: for each group g and column c, I'm storing the scaled quantization term a_gc, and for each group, the sum of scaled zero-point contributions b_g, so the final q_eff can be computed as a weighted sum minus the precomputed offset. The kernel layout has 8 warps processing 32 packed rows each across 256 total rows, with each thread handling 4 columns. Per iteration, a warp loads a packed row with its 4 columns (contiguous 4 bytes), performs 16 multiply-accumulates across 2 rows, then reduces within the warp using shuffle operations to produce 2 outputs. Now I'm checking that q_eff properly incorporates q_nope from the projection matrix and that rope is applied correctly to the query from that same source. For the output computation, I need to verify whether the reference implementation computes the exact same floating-point operations—the order matters for bf16 vs fp32 precision, especially since the reference dequantizes weights to fp32 before the key-value operations. For the attention computation across L-chunks, I'm reading from the cache buffers that include the newly appended row at position L, and the softmax runs over all positions from 0 to L. Each chunk block computes the scaled dot product between the effective query and cached key-values, then incorporates the rope-based attention scores. The kernel uses 256 threads organized as 32 heads × 8 slices to parallelize the 512-element dot products, with shuffle reductions across warp boundaries to aggregate scores per head. The cache loads are vectorized to improve memory coalescing when reading the 512-element bf16 slices. Processing all 256 l's serially might be slow—roughly 64 MACs per thread plus reduction overhead could take around 100 cycles per l, totaling ~25K cycles across all l's, but since 64 blocks run in parallel each handling their own 256 l's, the stage completes in about 12 microseconds. An alternative approach would be to treat the computation more like a GEMM, tiling a chunk of the cache into shared memory and having threads compute score blocks in parallel rather than serially. The math works out to roughly 537M MACs spread across ~150 SM blocks, which is about 14K MACs per thread—the theoretical minimum at one FMA per cycle per thread is around 7 microseconds at 2GHz. To push faster would require using lower precision (bf16 with HFMA2 for 2× throughput) or tensor cores, but for a first version accepting 7–10 microseconds is reasonable and can be optimized later. For the accumulator layout, I'm considering whether to keep the 64KB fp32 accumulator in shared memory alongside the 8KB staging for the key-value tile, which fits within the 80KB dynamic limit, or alternatively store it in registers where each thread holds 64 floats across a (head, 64-slice) partition. The key insight is that the same 64-element ckv slice used during the score computation can be reused for the accumulator update—if I align the thread mapping so that each thread handles the same 64 dimensions of both the dot product and the subsequent multiply-accumulate, I avoid redundant loads. For each sequence position, I load the ckv slice once into registers, compute the partial dot, reduce across 8 lanes to get the attention weight, then apply online softmax with the max correction factor. Register pressure is tight but manageable: the 64-element accumulator plus 32 packed ckv registers plus online softmax state (m and sumexp) fits within the 256 registers per thread available at 1 block per SM. The online softmax is maintained redundantly across all 8 lanes of each head—when the max updates, I rescale the existing accumulator and sumexp by the correction factor, then add the new weighted contribution. For the next phase, I'm writing partial results (m, sumexp, and the 512-element accumulator) per head to workspace memory as fp32 for numerical stability—about 4.2MB total across all blocks. Then in the combine phase, I read these partials back, compute the global maximum and rescaled sum across all chunks, normalize by the final sumexp, and apply the value projection using the same transposed GEMV pattern as before. Now I'm laying out the concrete architecture: d=2304 with K packed to 1152 and 18 groups, HDk=4096. I'm organizing the work into stages where each stage contains a set of tasks, and each block handles its assigned tasks in a loop. Now I'm working through the per-thread layout: 32 threads per warp × 4 columns each = 128 columns total per task. Each thread reads packed weights and accumulates partial sums across its column range, handling the fact that packed rows contain pairs of actual rows that share dequantization group indices. I'm refactoring the accumulation to group by quantization groups, computing the scaled dot products within each group and subtracting the zero-point contributions. The challenge is that my warp's K-slice (144 packed rows) doesn't align cleanly with group boundaries—each group spans 64 packed rows, so I'm getting 2.25 groups per warp. I need to split the computation into group-aligned chunks: process the first 64 packed rows as one complete group, the next 64 as another, then handle the remaining 16 rows as a partial group, restarting the inner dot product at each boundary. The key insight is that the scaling term s[g,c]·z[g,c]·gsum_g can be computed per subrange using just that subrange's partial sum—linearity lets me substitute gsum_sub for the full group sum since I'm only accumulating over the rows my warp actually processes. Now I'm working through how to precompute these partial sums efficiently in shared memory. The challenge is that warps don't align cleanly to group boundaries, so I need to either restructure the K-split so warps map to complete groups, or precompute a prefix-sum table of x values to handle partial overlaps. I'm exploring whether to use 6 or 9 warps per block to balance the group assignments across warps. Actually, I think the simpler approach is to skip the group-sum precomputation entirely and just compute the contribution directly per element: each thread multiplies x_k by the scaling factor s[g,c] and the weight term (w − z[g,c]) as it goes. But if I want to optimize with group sums, I need all warps to process aligned group chunks so gsum is computed consistently—assigning warp w to groups w, w+8, w+16, etc. For K=2304 this creates load imbalance where some warps handle 3 groups and others only 2, roughly 50% worse. The memory-bound GEMV dominates block time, so this imbalance within a block is tolerable. For computing gsum per group into shared memory, I can have the first 144 threads each sum 16 consecutive x values, then use atomic adds into shared memory to reduce into gsum[k/128]—144 atomics are fine. For the actual computation, t = x*inv*w where gsum is computed over t, and since t is only known after inv is available, I need two passes anyway: first pass sums x², second pass computes t and stores it in shared memory while accumulating per-group sums. For dot_gc, I'm extracting nibbles from packed 4-column, 2-row uint32 values, converting each to float, and doing FMA operations with the corresponding t values—roughly 6 operations per byte of input. The throughput requirement is about 10.8 TFLOPS at 1.8 TB/s, and GB202 can deliver around 62 TFLOPS of fp32 operations, so the math works but it's tight. I'm considering optimizations like LOP3 and PRMT tricks for faster int4-to-fp16 conversion, but the straightforward approach should work first. A simpler variant precomputes per-group-column values by factoring the dequantization as (n − z)*s = n*s − z*s, so I can accumulate as t_k * (n*a + b') where a and b' are precomputed constants. Now I'm realizing the conversion bottleneck is more severe than expected—extracting nibbles and converting int-to-float requires multiple operations per element, and the CVT throughput on Blackwell (around 16 per SM per clock) might not be sufficient for the full 14.4 trillion values per second needed at peak bandwidth. The ALU budget is getting squeezed. Let me recalculate more carefully: at 1.8TB/s of int4 data, that's 3.6 trillion values per second (two values per byte). Each value needs extraction (2 int ops), conversion (1 op), and FMA (1-2 ops depending on how I fold the dequantization), totaling roughly 5 operations per value. That works out to about 18 trillion operations per second, which is feasible on Blackwell's ~60-100 trillion ALU capacity—roughly 25% utilization. The key constraint is maintaining high instruction-level parallelism per thread, and I should explore using PRMT instructions to extract nibbles more efficiently than bit shifts and masks. There are also some clever conversion tricks from kernels like Marlin and AWQ: converting int4 values to fp16 or bf16 using magic numbers and LOP3 operations. For instance, the FasterTransformer approach uses a magic number like 0x6400 to construct fp16 values directly from nibbles, then subtracts an offset to recover the original value. With PRMT and LOP3, I can get down to about 1.3 operations per value to produce fp16 pairs, then use HFMA2 (half-precision FMA) with the scale and zero-point in bf16 format. The bf16 magic number approach is similar but accounts for bf16's narrower mantissa of only 7 bits. Now I'm working through the exact encoding: a 4-bit int (0-15) needs 4 mantissa bits to represent, and I'm checking whether bf16 0x4300 works as the base. With exponent bias 127, 0x4300 gives exponent 134 (2^7 = 128), and the mantissa ULP is 1, so I can encode 128 + n exactly for n < 16 by setting the low 4 mantissa bits. For the byte-pair trick with a packed u32 containing 4 columns × 2 rows, I'm using PRMT and bitwise operations to splat bytes into 16-bit lanes and form bf16 pairs—the layout is different from typical packing since we're handling 4 columns with 2 rows each rather than 8 values along a single dimension. Packing the bf16 values by shifting and ORing the nibbles, then using HFMA2 to accumulate pairs while keeping precision with bf16 accumulators. The dequantization scaling gets folded after accumulation per group, computing the contribution as the scaled sum minus the zero-point offset. Now I'm analyzing the numerical stability—bf16's 7-bit mantissa gives about 0.8% relative error per addition, and summing 64 values per lane across groups leads to roughly 6% error per group dot product. With the cosine tolerance allowing up to 14% relative error, the total error across all 18 groups stays in the 2-6% range when errors accumulate in quadrature. The challenge is that stacking multiple layers compounds these errors, which is risky. I can't use fp32 accumulation with HFMA2 on current hardware, and Blackwell doesn't have packed fp32 FADD2/FFMA2 either. A better approach might be to accumulate in bf16x2 only over short 16-row spans, then upconvert to fp32 accumulators—this keeps per-span error around 1%, which stays acceptable when the total variance is distributed across spans, with negligible impact on cosine similarity. Alternatively, I could use fp16 instead of bf16 since its 10-bit mantissa handles the magic (1024+n) trick exactly, and fp16 has enough dynamic range for small activations. The accumulation error drops to about 0.8% per group of 64 rows. But there's an overflow risk: products of (1024+n)*t can reach ~2000×|t|, and summing 64 of these could exceed fp16's 65504 limit, especially since the weights are initialized around N(0,0.02) and activations are O(1) in magnitude. For the first version, I'll stick with plain int extraction, convert to fp32, and use FMA operations—then measure performance. If the conversion becomes a bottleneck, I can revisit the fp16 trick. The CVT.S32.F32 throughput on SM120 is around 16 ops per clock per SM, which should handle the needed 3.6T conversions per second without saturating the pipeline. There's also a clever fp32 trick using bit manipulation—treating an integer as a float by ORing with 0x4B000000 to get 2^23 + n exactly—but the group correction approach runs into catastrophic cancellation issues. The error from computing gsum in fp32 relative precision (around 1e-7) scales up to absolute error of ~1 when multiplied by 8.4M, which is 1-10% of the dot product result. That's unacceptable unless I compute gsum exactly, which defeats the purpose of the trick. A better approach is to apply the magic subtract per-element: convert the integer to fp32 using the bit trick, then subtract 8388608 to recover the original value exactly. This avoids the CVT pipeline bottleneck entirely—just one integer OR, one float subtract, then FMA, all running on separate execution units. I could even pack two values per byte using nibbles to process them simultaneously. For extracting the 4-bit values from each packed u32, I'm using shift and AND operations per column—roughly 2 ops each, possibly optimizable with PRMT or LOP3 but probably not worth the complexity. That's about 5 ops per value across 3.6 trillion values, so around 18 teraops total, mostly integer shifts and ANDs at lower throughput. I should just implement this and profile it with NCU to see the actual performance. Now moving into stage C2 for the MLA score computation—the main ALU-heavy work at 16K. I'm doing fp32 FMA on bf16-converted loads, and recounting the MACs: with scores being (L+1)×32×(512+64) and accumulators (L+1)×32×512, at L=16400 that's roughly 572 million MACs, which should hit around 9 teraflops. For the bf16 to fp32 conversion of the c_kv values, each one gets used across all 32 heads, so if I cache a thread's 64-dim slice in registers as fp32 after converting once, I can reuse it—but the layout is tricky since threads handling different heads within a block all need the same c_kv slice, which means L1 cache hits for repeated loads and each thread converts independently. The conversion overhead is 512 values times 2 ops per head-thread group, and with 8 threads sharing a head across 256 total threads (32 heads × 8 lanes), the memory layout gets complex around which lane processes which dimension slice. Within a warp, lanes reading the same c_kv slice creates redundancy, but L1 broadcast handles it efficiently. I'm reconsidering the layout so each warp's 32 lanes cooperatively load different parts of the 512-element c_kv vector, with each lane responsible for 8 u32 values. The challenge is that head-dot operations then need values scattered across lanes, which creates shuffle overhead. Using shared memory to stage the c_kv tile lets threads load cooperatively with full coalescing, but the amplification from 32 heads reading the same elements from smem balloons bandwidth to ~1GB, which doesn't improve over the 17µs estimate. A better approach: each thread loads its 64-dim c_kv slice into registers once per l-iteration and reuses it for a single head computation. The 32× re-read is unavoidable with the current (L×512)@(512×32) matmul structure where each thread-group does 512-dot products. To eliminate re-reads, I'd need to tile over all 32 heads in registers simultaneously, but that requires 2048 values per thread—infeasible. The real solution is to use tensor cores with bf16 matrix operations: compute the scores GEMM where c_kv chunk (L_tile×512) multiplies q_eff^T (512×32) to produce L_tile×32 output, leveraging mma fragments for data reuse so the c_kv loads from L2/DRAM only once. For the accumulation step, I'd compute P^T @ c_kv using another tensor core GEMM, which is essentially flash-decoding with tensor cores—more involved but doable. A v1 estimate puts this stage around 15-20µs at 16K context, totaling maybe 200µs, so I'll defer that optimization. Alternatively, I could process multiple l's per thread without tensor cores by loading 4 c_kv slices at once, but that balloons register usage to 128 u32 values per thread, or I'd need each thread to handle two heads instead—trading off register pressure against reuse factor. Skipping the conversion and accumulating directly in bf16 over short spans risks precision loss, and fp16 doesn't work cleanly with bf16 c_kv. So I'll go with the tensor core approach: use m16n8k16 bf16 GEMM with fp32 accumulation, chunk the cache into 128-row blocks (padded and masked with −∞ scores), then compute the score matrix and attention output in one pass. The challenge is memory: q_eff needs to be transposed as a B operand (576×32 in bf16, about 36KB in shared memory), but staging the full chunk A matrix (128×576) into shared memory requires 147KB—way over budget. I need to k-slice the computation instead. For the actual computation: I'm storing the attention scores C as fp32 in registers (each warp holds its 16×32 block), then running online softmax across the block's 128 rows by reducing the max and sum per column through shared memory across all 8 warps. After that, I'm computing the final output by multiplying the softmax probabilities (transposed as 32×128) against the concatenated key-value matrix (128×512) using another mma operation. Each warp handles 16 n-tiles of a single m-tile, accumulating 2048 fp32 values across 64 registers per thread alongside fragment storage and staging indices—pushing close to 120 registers total but still feasible. Since each block processes exactly one chunk, I can skip online rescaling across chunks and just compute the full softmax numerator per chunk, which simplifies the logic considerably. For v1 I'll start with a scalar implementation, measure performance, and optimize if there's meaningful headroom to gain. Now looking at the rope embeddings in the score computation—q_rope is 32×64 fp32... Actually, let me step back and benchmark the whole pipeline first. At 2048 context the scalar path runs in about 2 microseconds, which is acceptable. The benchmark covers contexts of 2048, 8192, and 16384 with varying step counts, and the cache grows slightly over iterations but stays within the L+96 capacity. The real bottleneck at 16K is the baseline kv_b matmul—that's roughly 137 GFLOP with M=16K, K=512, N=8192, which likely takes 1-2 milliseconds alone on a bf16 GEMM. Our approach gets that down to about 0.2 milliseconds, which is a massive speedup at 16K, so I want to push all three context sizes in the score calculation. For MoE routing, the reference uses topk on the probabilities with k=8, and while tie-breaking order doesn't matter for the weights themselves, the indices have to match the reference selection exactly since different experts produce completely different outputs. The topk operation on fp32 softmax versus our fp32 exp should be fine unless we hit near-exact ties, which is negligible with random weights. I also need to remember the routed scaling formula: w = w/(w.sum()+1e-9)*2.446. The shared expert gets added without a weight applied. Let me verify the KDA o_proj input is correct — the output buffer is reshaped as H*Dk with head-major layout, which checks out. For beta_proj, the input x is the normalized t, and I'm computing y = t @ W_beta^T where the weight matrix is (32, 2304) in row-major order. This means each head's output is a dot product over the contiguous rows, which is efficient with one block and warps handling the computation — 32 heads across 2304 dimensions with 256 threads per block means 8 warps each handling 4 heads, doing lane-strided reductions over the 2304 elements. The router follows a similar linear projection pattern. For the router's (2304→64) projection with W as (64, 2304) row-major, I'm computing 64 logits plus softmax and selecting the top-8 experts. One block reading 295KB is tight — that's about 3-6 microseconds with vectorized loads at 50-100GB/s throughput. I could split this across 8 blocks (one per 8 heads) but that requires synchronization between stages, which defeats the purpose. Keeping it all in one block with 256 threads is the simpler approach, though the memory bandwidth is the bottleneck here. A better idea: compute the router logits in stage E (shared across 8 blocks writing different logit groups), then have each stage F block redundantly compute its own top-8 from the full 64 logits — that's just 256 bytes in L2 cache and ~200 cycles of work per block, with no extra synchronization needed. The redundant softmax and top-k per block is cheap enough to avoid the coordination overhead. Stage E handles the shared g/u computation plus router logits, and stage F blocks independently pick their experts by task ID using their locally-computed top-8. The beta dependency is simpler — computed in stage B, consumed in stage C, no issues there. Now I need to count the barrier synchronizations. Precomputing a combined weight matrix would triple the memory traffic, so that's not viable. The 16K path looks manageable with C2's cache and compute dominating. I'll stick with 26 grid syncs and structure the code to support swapping in arrive/wait flags later for finer-grained synchronization. Now I need to figure out the workspace buffer sizes on device. Looking at the memory requirements for different stages — the C2 stage with scalar operations needs minimal shared memory, while the GEMV stages require more space for intermediate reductions. With a shared memory budget around 20KB, I can fit 4+ blocks per SM, though register pressure will realistically cap it at 2-3 blocks. I'm settling on the occupancy-based grid size, capped at 512 blocks, and mapping tasks to blocks per stage using grid-stride enumeration. For the KDA stage C specifically, I have 32 tasks (one per head). Each block handles reading its convolution window slices, computing the convolution outputs with SiLU activation, and shifting the window buffer. Then it computes the decay factor using a numerically stable softplus formulation that handles large values correctly. The beta values are already sigmoid-transformed in stage B, so I just read them from the buffer. For the S matrix update, I'm using a two-pass approach where threads are distributed across the columns—each thread handles one column of the dv dimension, with the upper bit determining which half of the reduction it participates in. In the first pass, each thread sums over its assigned rows of S, multiplying by precomputed decay and k values stored in shared memory. The two halves then combine their partial results. I'm precomputing decay×k and other products like beta×k into shared memory arrays to avoid redundant loads during the row iterations. In the second pass, I'm updating S with the decay factor and adding the contribution from beta×k scaled by delta, then accumulating into the output partial. Delta itself is computed in shared memory after pass 1 as the difference between v and the predicted values. I'm storing the updated S values and reading them again from L1 cache on the second access, which should mostly hit since the 64KB working set fits within the 128KB L1 per SM. The precision looks solid too — using fp32 for S with bf16 inputs introduces only tiny rounding differences in the cosine similarity, well within acceptable bounds. Now for the MLA architecture details: I'm organizing 32 head-specific tasks plus one additional task for rope operations and cache updates. Each head task handles the non-rope q components, while rope operations on q get folded into the computation. The append task manages copying the key-value buffer into the cache row and applying rope to the rope-specific k dimensions, converting between fp32 and bf16 as needed. For C3, I'm gathering partial results across chunks (up to 65) to compute the global max and normalized attention weights, then applying the value projection. Each head processes its partials by reading across chunks and accumulating the weighted context, which gets multiplied by the transposed value weight matrix using int4 GEMV operations. The memory footprint for these partials across all 32 head blocks comes to around 4.3MB in DRAM. The softmax computation spans all L+1 positions across chunks, combining the per-chunk exponential sums with the global max for numerical stability. The kv_a output stores cache in bf16 format, which matches the reference implementation's precision after accounting for fp32 accumulation differences. Now I'm setting up the CUDA kernel structure with a params struct that organizes all the per-layer weights and configuration — handling the different layer types (KDA variants and MLA), with pointers to quantized weights, scales, zeros, and expert parameters for the MoE layers. Defining the weight structures... Defining kernel parameters... Writing device kernel signature... I'm settling on a tile size of 128 columns with 32 threads across, giving me 8 warps per block that each handle a K-partition of the group range. Now I need to verify all the matrix dimensions are compatible with this 128-column tile size—most work out cleanly, but I'll need to handle the 576-column case with a tail tile using masked columns for the remaining 64 columns. The task structure will include the matrix dimensions, column offset, number of columns in the tile, group range, and pointers, with each stage's task list built via constexpr arithmetic rather than runtime code generation. For load balancing, I'm distributing groups round-robin across the 8 warps, which creates some imbalance when the group count isn't a multiple of 8, but that's acceptable. Each warp then processes its assigned groups in the inner loop, handling 64 packed rows per group. Extracting the nibble shifts for each column, then after the group accumulation I'm reconsidering the cost of the z-folding approach versus using a precomputed group sum to avoid the per-element subtraction, which saves operations by keeping the magic offset baked into the accumulation instead of subtracting it out separately. Each warp handles all 128 columns of the tile, with threads accumulating partial sums across the K dimension, then reducing within shared memory using 8 warps writing to a 128×8 buffer before final aggregation. The total shared memory footprint is around 21KB per block, accounting for the full K accumulation, group sums, and reduction buffer, though these stages can be overlapped. For the RMSNorm and input loading phase, I'm deciding how to fill shared memory based on the operation: some layers need RMSNorm applied to the hidden state using layer norm weights, others load raw fp32 buffers, and a few compute fused operations like SILU multiplication on the fly from two separate buffers. Each fill pass also accumulates a sum for normalization. At the block level, after synchronizing threads post-fill, each task runs its inner GEMV operation. When multiple tasks share the same input within a grid-stride pattern, I can either refill shared memory each time or cache it if tasks are assigned strategically—refilling is cheap enough for smaller K dimensions since the data stays L2-hot. For expert selection in stages F and G, I need the top-k indices from the logits computed in stage E. Each block redundantly calculates the same top-8 selection from identical logits, which guarantees determinism across all blocks since the floating-point operations are identical. I also need per-expert weights for the scaled-atomic accumulation into the output, computed in that same redundant routine. The KDA C stage and other special stages are coded individually. At initialization, I upload parameters to constant memory using load_inline, which compiles a single module per symbol. For multi-context support, I'm sizing the workspace partials for the maximum context length encountered—allocating fixed space for 65 chunks handles the typical case, with lazy growth if a larger context appears. The capacity buffer gets allocated on import, which is the first step of execution. For import handling in-kernel, if the import flag is set, I copy the rows using grid-stride across layer 0's stage B with dedicated tasks appended—roughly 257 tasks for the 18.9MB of data, which stretches stage B only during import steps. The k_rope import uses 64 rows of bf16 at 128 bytes each. I'm doing a correctness check on state identity to verify the solution. py output matches the reference: the c_kv tensor shape is correct, imported rows are exact copies, and the new row is computed properly. The cosine comparison in check.py dominates over the identical portion, which is fine. The benchmark gates correctness with a single-step check before timing, and the timed runs don't validate output but must not crash due to cache capacity constraints. Comparing memory traffic between chunk sizes: with 64-byte chunks I get around 52MB total (including partial writes and reads), while 256-byte chunks drop that to 27MB. The tradeoff is that smaller chunks only keep 65 of the 296 blocks busy, so even though compute per block is substantial (around 34K cycles per thread), I'm only utilizing a fraction of the hardware and might still be bandwidth-limited despite the lower traffic volume. The error grows with the square root of the number of rows, while the signal grows much more slowly, so the signal-to-noise ratio degrades significantly. The core issue is that the +128 offset required for the bit-manipulation magic in bf16 is actually destroying the accumulation precision. I could try using a smaller offset, but the bf16 format constraints don't allow it—with only a 7-bit mantissa, the minimum offset that gives unit least-significant-bit precision is exactly 128. However, fp16 with its 10-bit mantissa could use an offset of 1024 instead, which would give much better numerical behavior for the accumulation. But that creates a different problem: the offset 1024 is now catastrophically large compared to the signal values (which are in the 0–15 range). So I need to pre-subtract the magic offset before accumulating—applying a half-precision subtraction to remove the 128 (or 1024) offset from each pair of values, then accumulating the corrected small values. This adds a few extra operations per pair, but keeps the accumulation working with reasonable magnitudes. The tricky part is that the zero-point varies per column, so I can't fold it into a constant subtraction—I'd need to handle it dynamically. The better approach is to precompute the zero-point offset per column at the start of each group, add 128 to it, and splat it across a half-precision pair. Then subtract that from the input before accumulating, which centers the values around zero and keeps the partial sums stable. The differences (n − z) stay exact in bf16 since both are small integers, and the products with the rounded input values accumulate as a zero-mean random walk. During accumulation, I'm grouping the t2 pairs accordingly: the low accumulator gets contributions from pairs 0 and 2, while the high accumulator gets pairs 1 and 3. At flush time, I convert both accumulators to float32 and sum them, with the high accumulator divided by 16 to account for the weight scaling. For the z-centering, I'm precomputing two fp16x2 constants per column per group: one for the unscaled case (1024+z) and one for the scaled case (1024+16z). At the start of each group, I convert z to float, then create these two constants using float-to-half2 conversion—just a few operations per group per column that let me subtract the appropriate centered value from each pair. Now looking at the per-u32 loop: each iteration processes 8 values from one column with a load, shift, four logical operations, four half-precision subtractions, and four fused multiply-adds, plus occasional shared memory loads. The accumulator needs flushing every 16 iterations to stay within fp16 headroom (around 5.8K), which adds about 4 operations per u32. For the integer tally, I'm using a shift, four logical operations, and an add per 4-byte chunk. Checking the throughput: the integer path hits 1.5 operations per byte, which translates to 12 trillion operations per second at 8TB/s bandwidth—well under the 17-34 trillion cap. For the fp16 pipeline, the half-precision operations deliver 2 operations per byte, reaching 16 trillion operations per second. The B200's fp16 CUDA core rate is roughly double fp32, and with HFMA2 instructions executing 128 per SM per clock (each doing 2 operations), that's about 34.7 trillion HFMA2 instructions per second. My instruction rate of 16 trillion per second is around 46% of that capacity, which checks out. Now I'm thinking about how to combine integer and floating-point work to hit maybe 5-6.5 TB/s of streaming throughput. For shared memory loads, if each thread handles two columns per row block, I can amortize the four t2 loads across registers for better reuse. I'm restructuring the inner loop to load two columns and then do eight lop3 operations. The idea is to have each thread process 64 columns (2 per thread), which means a 128-column tile would span 2 warps. I'm considering an 8-warp configuration where each warp handles 64 columns across K-split slices, giving me a 64×8 tile. For coalescing, I need 32 threads accessing consecutive u32 values for adjacent columns. The layout for w_r is (K/8, N) in u32 format, where each warp row reads from two 128-byte segments—one at (rb, c0+lane) and another at (rb, c0+32+lane)—which gives perfect coalescing. I'm recounting the shared memory broadcast loads: for each row block, the t2 values load once per column pass, and with pragma unroll and both columns in registers, that's 4 shared memory loads plus 2 global loads, then 2 shifts, 4 lop3 operations, 4 hsub2 operations, and 4 hfma2 operations per row block per thread. The compiler should hoist the t2 loads across both columns. Now I need to figure out whether the transposed GEMVs for qeff and Wv·ctx can use the original w_q layout. For the transposed GEMV where we iterate over rows as output (out[r] = Σ_c w[r,c]×qn[c]), I'm repacking kv_b into a transposed layout where each warp handles one row block. The new kvbT layout is (K, N/8) in u32 format, with element (r, cb) containing the nibbles of columns 8cb through 8cb+7 at row r in FT pair order. For the q_eff head block, I'm iterating over rows with 32 lanes per thread producing 256 columns, but since each head only has 128 columns, I'd only use 16 lanes—one u32 per 8 columns, so 16 u32 values for the 128 columns in head h. I need to repack per head. Now I'm arranging kvbT_k as [head][r][16 u32] and multiplying by qn2 pairs (fp16x2 of q_nope in permuted order, computed at runtime by C1 which converts q_nope from fp32 to qn2 in shared memory with FT pair permutation). Per row r, each lane executes a load, shift, and fused multiply-add sequence to cover the computation, and I'm assigning 2 rows per warp step—lanes 0-15 handle row r while lanes 16-31 handle row r+1. The layout is row-major with 512 rows × 16 u32. After the fused multiply-add over 16 lanes per row, I need to reduce the 16 lane accumulators and handle dequantization with per-lane z-centering based on column groups, where z2/zb2 values are loaded once per group across the 512-row dimension. Now I'm realizing the z and scale factors vary per column within each lane's 8 columns, so I need four z-pairs per lane to handle the column grouping. The scale factor s depends on which column group we're in, so I'm precomputing scaled quantization values qn2 variants—one for each of the four row groups—and switching between them at group boundaries using a shared memory lookup table. For the dequantization output, I can fold the constant bias term: instead of computing the full (v−z) subtraction per element, I'm precomputing b_g = Σ_c s_c z_c qn_c as a single scalar per group, then subtracting it once from the accumulated raw values. This eliminates the need for HSUB entirely—I just accumulate the raw magic values (1024+v) scaled by sqn, then subtract the precomputed bias at the end. But now I'm hitting a precision problem: the offset (1024×sqn) dominates the signal (v×sqn) by about 146×, and with fp16 accumulation across ~8 terms, the relative error balloons to roughly 20%, which is unacceptable. So I need to keep the z-centering with HSUB2 to keep intermediate values small, then fold the scaled qn2s values per u32 using a shift, four lop3 operations, four hsub2 operations, and four hfma2 operations. For the hsub2 arguments, I'm precomputing z2 arrays indexed by column pair and group—storing (z_cj, z_cj+4) as fp16x2 pairs—and laying them out as a 4×64 fp16x2 table (2KB per SM) so each lane can quickly fetch the z-values it needs for its column subset. Then during the main loop, I accumulate even and odd results separately across the 8-column dot products, flush per row, and reduce across 16 lanes using shuffles to combine the fp32 accumulators into a single output value per lane—roughly 12 operations per row amortized, which keeps the per-head workload manageable across 512 rows split among 8 warps. For the folding step, I'm converting q_nope from fp32 to fp16 via rounding before multiplying with the scale values. The same approach applies to the Wv^T context multiplication in the next stage, just reading from a different column range of the head. The MoE layers follow standard GEMV patterns with K=1024 for the gate/up projections split into 18 groups and K=4096 for the output projection across 32 groups. Conv and miscellaneous stages use bf16 reads throughout. For the initial version, I'm going to keep things simple—stick with fp32 magic path, natural weight layout, no repacking—to get correctness working first, then optimize the inner loop in a second pass. Looking at the performance ceiling: the fp32 magic approach with natural u32 layout gives roughly 3TB/s throughput, which translates to around 70µs for GEMV stages plus another 40µs for the rest, totaling about 110µs. The optimized v2 with repacked fp16 and LOP3 should hit around 40µs, but v1's 110µs is acceptable as a baseline. I'll keep v1 as a compile-time fallback and replace the inner loop entirely in v2 rather than trying to share code paths between the two layouts. For the output handling, I'm applying alpha (the expert weight or shared scaling factor) at the atomicAdd stage. On the memory side, I need to store the task vector in shared memory in two formats: fp16 pairs for the GEMV operation in FT order, plus an fp32 array for beta, router, and gradient sum calculations. That's roughly 24KB of shared memory accounting for alignment. For v1, I'll keep everything in fp32 to simplify the implementation. Before writing the full solution, I should benchmark the cost of grid synchronization since I'm planning to use cooperative groups with 27+ barriers—that could become a bottleneck. I'll write a quick microbenchmark kernel to measure grid.sync overhead on this GPU, then use those results to inform the final design.
