The synchronization overhead for grid barriers is around 1.2-1.6 microseconds depending on grid size, so 27 barriers total roughly 32 microseconds—meaningful relative to the 40-60 microsecond compute target but workable for now, though I could optimize this later with merged stages or custom barriers that might shave it down to 0.5-0.8 microseconds. Now I'm writing the solution.py v1 with the core GEMV kernel: each warp processes a subset of quantization groups across 128-column tiles with 256 threads per block (8 warps), loading scale and zero-point values as bfloat16 pairs and accumulating results with dequantization applied per group. For edge cases like the kv_a tail with fewer than 128 columns, I'm guarding loads to skip out-of-bounds accesses while still participating in reductions, and handling the row-major addressing with stride 576 for the full matrix layout. I'm verifying alignment: all N dimensions (4096, 2304, 6144, 576, 8192, 1024, 32, 64) are multiples of 4, and column indices computed as col0 + 4*lane are naturally u32-aligned. For the beta projection layer with N=32 (bfloat16 row-major), I'm treating it as a separate task type alongside the router with N=2304. Writing the main loop structure... The prologue stage needs to zero out the qkvg buffer before the barrier, and I'm planning to zero gacc and uacc during stage C using a few dedicated tasks, while the qkvg zeroing for the next layer gets appended to stage E. The output buffer doesn't need zeroing since stage C writes to it with single ownership per head task. So I need to zero gacc/uacc before stage E, which I can do during stage C/C2—KDA has plenty of tasks in stage C, and for MLA I can use stage C1 plus a few extra tasks from the C2 range to handle the zeroing. Both happen before stage E anyway, so that works out. Stage E's shared GEMV then uses Ksplit atomics into the gacc slot with 18 groups splitting into 2, and stage F routes into slots 0-7. For the MoE down GEMVs, I'm looking at K=1024 across 8 groups, with Ksplit producing 2 splits per expert—36 tasks in the shared path and 8×36 in the routed path. Now for stage D's o_proj with K=4096 and 32 groups: I'm splitting into 4 Ksplits of 8 groups each, giving 18 tiles per split for 72 tasks total. But I need to account for the fact that qkvg is still being read in stage D—the input comes from obuf, and qkvg was last read in stage C. For KDA that's q, k, v, g; for MLA it's q in C1 and kv in C1 with append. So qkvg should be zeroed in D, except stage B of MLA writes q and kv totaling 6720 floats, meaning I need to zero the union region of 16416 floats. That brings stage D to 72 + 9 = 81 tasks, and if I push to Ksplit 8 I'd get 144 or more. For stage E with the router, I'm exploring task granularity around bf16 dot products of 2304 per row. A single block could handle all 64 rows with 8 warps processing 8 rows each, where each warp-row is 72 bf16 values per lane. One task reading roughly 295KB with 256 threads and unrolling should take around 0.15 microseconds. Now I'm settling on 2 router tasks with 32 rows each. Stage E totals 34 tasks: 16 shared gate tiles with 2 splits, 16 shared up, 2 router, and no obuf-zero since the next layer's C is single-owner. For stage F, I'm calculating per-expert costs: 16 gate tiles with 2 splits plus 16 up gives 32 tasks per expert, times 8 experts is 256, plus 36 shared down tasks, totaling around 292 tasks. Stage G handles the routed down with 288 tasks, then after the final barrier I'm writing a single epilogue task that converts the hidden buffer to bf16 and outputs it. The key thing I'm tracking is that qkvg gets zeroed in each layer's stage D after stage C consumes it, ensuring the next layer's stage B has clean values to work with. Now I'm tracking the workspace layout for the MLA qkvg buffer: q occupies the first 6144 positions across 96 tasks (48 tiles with 2 splits each), while kv takes positions 6144-6720 across 10 tasks (5 tiles with 2 splits). The import logic only runs at layer 0 for the KDA kernel, where import tasks copy the checkpoint data into the capacity buffer and rope embeddings. For the import copy operation, I'm sizing each task to handle 64 rows, which means 257 total tasks each copying about 74KB of data using uint4 operations for efficiency. The grid-stride approach uses 296 blocks with 7 tasks per block to manage this workload. Moving into MLA's C1 stage with 33 tasks, where the first 32 tasks handle the head computation including rope embeddings for the query. For the rope computation itself, I'm processing pairs of elements (even, odd) per thread across a warp, computing the rotation using the standard formula with inverse theta values, then applying cosine and sine transformations with proper scaling before storing as bf16. The precision should be fine since the cosf/sinf operations match torch's reference implementation, though I'm noting that large position values up to 16420 radians could affect cosine accuracy. Now for the qeff gemv operation, I'm laying out a transposed matrix multiplication where 8 warps each handle 32 pairs of rows, with each lane covering 4 columns. The memory access pattern loads 128 bytes coalesced per pair, and I'm accumulating products of quantized values scaled by group-dependent factors that I'll precompute per warp. For each lane's 4 values, I reload them at group boundaries and compute both the main accumulation and a weighted sum across the group using warp reduction. The final qeff result combines these reductions: the sum of element-wise products minus the scaled group sum, computed once per group boundary to avoid redundant work. Now I'm handling the output formatting—converting the computed values to lower precision with a scale factor, then storing them to the output buffer. I'm also preparing the source data for the next phase: loading the query-key-value group data and setting up the rope encoding for position-based rotations across the sequence length. For the cache storage, I'm keeping the raw k_rope values without folding in the scale factor since the scale gets applied separately during the query-rope computation, which matches the reference implementation. The c_kv cache also stays raw since the scaling is already baked into the effective query values. Next I'm zeroing out the gradient and update accumulators, then organizing the computation into chunks of 64 rows for the second phase of the multi-head latent attention, where each task processes a specific range of rows within the sequence. Now I'm working through the thread layout and memory access patterns for the kernel, distributing heads across passes and organizing how the cached key-value data and query-rope values get loaded into registers for the scoring computation. Checking register pressure with accumulator, query efficiency, and temporary buffers. Handling edge cases for chunks beyond the context length. At 2048 context, 33 chunks fit cleanly into 33 blocks. For the MLA C3 phase with 32 head tasks, I'm doing a block-level reduction to find the max of the partial max values, then computing the denominator by summing scaled partial sums, and finally reconstructing the output by accumulating scaled partial results across all chunks with thread-level stride access. For the exponential computation, I'm precomputing the exponential values once in shared memory since we have at most 260 chunks (fits in ~1KB), then reusing those values across all dimension threads to avoid redundant exponential calls. After that, I'm doing the transposed matrix-vector multiply with the context vector to produce the output buffer, similar to the query efficiency path but without scale folding. The MLA D-G projection follows the same structure as the KDA MoE path since the output projection has identical shapes. For Stage C KDA, I'm setting up the convolution computation where each thread loads a query channel result, applies the convolution window taps across 3×128 channels, and produces the output by combining the window values with the learned weights. reasoning
The errors are massive—hundreds of magnitude—but the cosine is 0.937, which suggests the problem might be an additive error rather than a scaling issue. The values are wildly off (303 vs 1.25, roughly a 240x ratio), and I'm wondering if the zero correction based on gsum has the wrong sign or magnitude. If I remove that z*gsum term entirely, the error becomes just the sum of s_g × z_g × gsum_g, which is still large. Working through the math: with z averaging around 7.5, s around 0.005, and gsum per group being roughly the sum of 64 values (now 128 elements per group, so gsum halves), the total error across 18 groups should be around ±2.7 RMS—nowhere near 300. That massive discrepancy suggests something else is broken. The factor between 303 and 1.25 is suspiciously close to 2^23, which makes me think the magic subtraction for the nibble values isn't working correctly. The compiler might be reassociating the FMA operation—fusing (a - 8388608.f) × t + d in a way that destroys the n×t contribution when a = 2^23 + n, because the mantissa of a*t gets rounded and loses the lower bits entirely. The fix is to prevent that reassociation: use rounding-specified intrinsics like __fsub_rn to compute the subtraction exactly, then feed that into the FMA without letting the compiler contract them back together. Now I'm checking whether this is actually the culprit by looking at the error magnitudes — the observed errors up to 800 seem plausible given the accumulation of rounding errors across thousands of terms, each contributing roughly ±0.5 ULP scaled by the input values. But wait, if the error before scaling is around 60,000 and then gets multiplied by s (0.005), that gives 300, which matches what I'm seeing. Let me recalculate the accumulation more carefully: with a ≈ 2^23 and 64 terms, the partial sum reaches around ±9×10^7, where the ULP is 8, so each addition introduces rounding error around 4, and across a half-group that compounds to roughly 45 per group. The key question is whether d0 is computed as a contracted sum of all a×t terms without intermediate subtractions, then later subtracted from z×gsum as a single operation. The scaled error estimate is still way too small compared to the observed 300, so I need to test this directly—either recompile without fast math flags to see what the compiler actually does, or manually compute what a single column produces. The safer approach is to rewrite the bit extraction to use an explicit integer-to-float conversion that can't be contracted by the compiler, converting the extracted nibble directly to float rather than relying on floating-point arithmetic that might get fused in unexpected ways. The real issue is that `--use_fast_math` enables not just fast transcendentals but also aggressive FMA contraction and reassociation—so expressions like `(X − C) * t + d` might get rewritten as a fused multiply-add that changes the numerical result. Using an explicit `(float)(int)` cast for the nibble extraction bypasses this entirely and gives me a clean, uncontractable conversion to work with. But the bigger red flag is that beta itself was wrong even without int4 involved—just pure bf16 × t in the dense rows—which points to the rmsnorm computation `t` being the actual culprit. If `t` is off, then `q` and `cos` would both be affected equally. I should isolate and test the rmsnorm error separately to confirm. Looking at the block_sum function in the fill_norm_bf16 kernel, there's a potential race condition: it writes to shared memory and then syncs, but the synchronization pattern might not be protecting all the reads properly. The issue is that only threads in warp 0 (tid 0-7) actually read the valid summed values from the red array after the warp_sum operation. When the shuffle broadcasts within each warp, threads in warps 1-7 are broadcasting zeros since their r values were never set to anything meaningful — they only participated in the initial read where r was set to 0 for tid >= 8. So block_sum only returns the correct total for warp 0, while the other warps get zeros. This cascades into the normalization: threads in warps 1-7 compute inv = rsqrt(0/D + eps) = 1000, so when they scale their t values in the fill loop, they're off by a factor of 1000. That's why I'm seeing the q values and cosines don't match expectations — about 7/8 of the elements are scaled incorrectly. The fix is to have thread 0 write the final sum to red[0], sync, then have all threads read from that single location. Fixing block_sum...

reasoning
I found the bug: `block_sum` broadcasts the total only within warp 0, leaving warps 1–7 with 0 and scaling most of the vector incorrectly. I'll fix this by broadcasting through shared memory instead.

I'm narrowing down the issue to three possibilities: either there's a bug in how the mixture-of-experts top-k function is computing expert indices or weights, there could be a race condition where stage F is reading stale logits, or the task-to-slot mapping calculation might be incorrect. Looking at the task decomposition more carefully — each slot maps to 32 tasks split across 2 materials, 8 tiles, and 2 splits — and the expert weight offsets are computing correctly for the quantized weights and scales. But the gate value of 0.90 paired with an up value of 0.55 is suspicious; if the expert index were completely wrong, the cosine similarity should be near zero, so this partial match suggests some tiles might be pulling from the right expert while others pull from the wrong one, pointing to either a tile-level mix-up or stale shared memory from a previous layer's computation. The topk implementation is using a warp-level reduction with proper descending order sorting and argmax logic via shuffle operations, so the ordering should match the reference implementation — but I need to verify whether the logits buffer itself is stable across blocks or if there's some race condition in how it's being read during stage F. Actually, I'm spotting a potential synchronization issue: after the loop writes selected indices and weights to shared memory, the code reads those values back without a `__syncwarp()` barrier, which on Volta and newer architectures could cause some lanes to see stale shared memory values if they're reading what other lanes just wrote. Let me verify if topk is actually producing wrong results by checking the downstream gate values against the reference — I'm seeing partial cosine similarity matches (0.9 vs 0.5), which suggests the topk indices might be mostly correct but some thread blocks are computing different top-k selections. Since all blocks use the same logits and deterministic code, they should produce identical results unless there's uninitialized shared memory being read. Looking more carefully at the F stage where `moe_topk` runs conditionally — it has `__syncthreads()` inside, but that should be fine since `topk_done` is set when entering the task loop. The gate output indexing looks correct too. I'm now focusing on the error pattern in gate[0] with that 0.9 cosine similarity — it might be just one tile causing the mismatch. The shared-down blocks are reading from slot 8 correctly after the barrier, and there's no race since the routed tasks only write to slots 0-7. I should verify the topk indices by running a probe after stage F, then comparing the actual moeacc values against what I'd compute from the logits in Python—checking each slot against all 64 experts to see which one was actually written will tell me if the indices are wrong or if the values got corrupted.

Let me trace through the task bit layout to verify the extraction logic is correct: the task encoding uses slot (3 bits), material (1 bit), tile (3 bits), and split (1 bit), so extracting the material with `rem >> 4` should work, tile with `(rem >> 1) & 7` should work, and split with `rem & 1` should work. But I'm wondering if there's a synchronization issue in the shared memory where the expert indices are being written—specifically whether the condition for writing to shared memory is actually executing on the right lanes, or if there's a mismatch between which lane writes and which lane reads the expert index. reasoning
The C2 partials are working correctly, so the issue must be in C3—either in the combine function or the W_v GEMV operation. I need to verify whether I actually updated mla_combine to use gemv_i4 instead of gemv_i4_T when I patched the call sites, so let me check the current file to see if that change was applied. Looking at the actual code, I realize the patch script only handled the out-pointer changes but didn't fix mla_combine itself. It's still calling gemv_i4_T with the wrong dimensions—reading only 128 context values when it should read 512, and writing 512 outputs that overflow into adjacent heads' memory regions. That's exactly what's causing the L3.o garbage. The fix is to replace that call with a proper matrix-vector multiply that computes each output element as the sum of all 512 context values weighted by the corresponding W column. I'll implement this inline in mla_combine without needing SmemG—just a dedicated loop that processes the 512 rows and 128 output columns, grouping by half-blocks to match the gemv_i4 pattern. The simplest approach is to reuse the gemv_i4 structure by copying the context data into the shared memory region where the temporary storage lives, since SmemM and the gemv union overlap in memory. But there's another issue: gemv_i4 uses atomicAdd to write outputs, which means it's adding to whatever was already in obuf from the previous layer's KDA stage instead of overwriting it. I need to either zero obuf first or switch to plain stores. The cleanest approach is to zero obuf during the C1 stage of the next layer—I can add two zero tasks (42 and 43) since layer 2's stage D finishes before layer 3's C1 begins, making it safe to clear. Then I'll update the mla_combine tail and adjust the C1 task count accordingly.
assistant
reasoning
I found the bug in `mla_combine` — it's calling the transposed GEMV for W_v·ctx which causes a shape mismatch and overwrites other heads. I'll fix it by using the standard GEMV over the 512 latent rows and zeroing `obuf` in C1 since the GEMV accumulates atomically.

reasoning
I'm seeing an 11x speedup which is good, but the throughput is still way off—0.78-0.83 ms/tok when I'm targeting 60-100µs. The weight floor and barriers account for maybe 60µs combined, so something else is creating a major bottleneck. The GEMV inner loop might be ALU-bound and only hitting around 3TB/s throughput, which would give roughly 70µs, but that doesn't explain the 780µs gap. I need to dig into occupancy and other factors to find what's causing this pathological slowdown. My leading hypothesis is that the `#pragma unroll 8` isn't actually unrolling the inner loop, leaving loads serialized with N-stride—meaning each warp only has one load in flight at a time. With 148 blocks, 8 warps per block, and 128B per load, that's only about 150KB in flight, which would tank throughput to 0.2TB/s and easily explain the 700µs latency for 213MB of data. I need to profile this with NCU to confirm, so I'm going to check register usage and occupancy from ptxas, time a single kernel with torch profiler and CUDA events, and then run NCU on one step to see what's actually happening.

Then I shift w right by 8 to process the upper half, repeating the same masking pattern for p2 and p3. This gives me four packed fp16 values with just one shift and four LOP3 operations. Now I need to double-check the fp16 GEMV precision: the per-operation relative error is around 0.3–0.7%, which feeds into convolution, state updates, and attention. The v1 baseline had fp32 exactness with cosine similarities around 0.9946–0.9999, so adding ~0.5% noise per GEMV might push hidden-state cosines to around 0.995 or so—still acceptable. But seed 0 is puzzling: it's already at 0.9946 in v1 despite exact fp32 GEMVs, while seeds 1 and 2 hit 0.9999. The only differences from reference should be fp32-vs-bf16 rounding, fp32 residuals in the hidden buffer, bf16 partial accumulation in MLA, and operation ordering. This suggests seed 0 has some specific sensitivity—maybe a near-tie in a top-k expert selection that flips which expert gets used, causing downstream divergence. The real risk is that if seed 0 is already at that threshold due to a topk flip, adding GEMV noise could trigger more expert flips on other seeds or contexts. I'm investigating whether the top-k indices actually differ from reference for seed 0. If it's a router tie-flip, that's inherent to how we compute logits: we're using fp32 logits from unrounded t2, while reference uses bf16-rounded t2, and that ~0.4% difference in logits can flip near-ties. Since an expert flip changes the output significantly, I should reduce this risk by rounding t2 to bf16 before the router dot product to match reference more closely. It's a cheap fix using bf16 in the dense_rows computation. Considering fp16 rounding for the projection inputs introduces only 0.05% error, which is acceptable when combined with other effects. Now I'm looking at stage C performance — the KDA state computation with its memory access patterns across multiple blocks. The second pass re-reads the state matrix from L1/L2 cache, and with the measured throughput around 0.5TB/s across 11-19µs, the bottleneck is actually latency rather than bandwidth. Each thread executes 64 dependent iterations with 512-byte strides, creating a long dependency chain. I'm exploring whether loading the state matrix as float4 vectors instead of individual floats would help — this way each thread processes four consecutive columns at once, reducing the number of loads needed and potentially getting more loads in flight to hide latency. The restructured approach uses 8-row slices per thread, with each thread handling a group of 4 columns via float4 loads. This increases the in-flight data per block from 4KB to around 32KB, which should push throughput closer to 1.4TB/s. I'm also considering whether to skip the intermediate pass structure entirely and instead use shared memory reduction across the 8 row-slices to compute the delta directly. Refactoring the kernel... ~1.5µs; then running through stages E through G gives me another 4+2.5µs each, plus the three C substages totaling around 12µs. Summing up the barrier overhead and Python overhead, I'm looking at roughly 101µs total for an 8K context—that's about an 88× speedup over the 8.9ms baseline. I could squeeze out another 15µs by optimizing the barrier reduction, so let me start implementing this approach. The atomicAdd operations per gemv tile are hitting 128 atomics per block, but contention should be negligible there. I'm also noticing that the gemv kernel's unroll pragma over 32 iterations with pointer arithmetic is creating the same stride pattern as before—with the repacking, addresses jump by N*4 bytes per read buffer. For stages B and F, I should consider reducing the K-splits; with prefetch pipelines in place, more tasks per block would amortize the fill cost better. Right now B is running 256 tasks across 296 blocks where each block fills shared memory for just one task, and that fill overhead—reading roughly 9KB of data—might be what's dragging down stage E's performance. The fill operation is actually making two passes over the input vector: one for computing the sum of squares and another for scaling, plus synchronization barriers between them. I can optimize this by doing a single pass: load the vector into shared memory while accumulating the sum of squares in the same step, then scale in-place from L1 cache afterward. This eliminates the redundant global memory round-trip and lets me compute the scaling factor simultaneously. I should also try loading the input via float4 vectors to reduce the number of transactions needed. For the dot product with bfloat16, I'll use the __hfma2 intrinsic for bfloat16 pairs (available in CUDA's bf16 header for sm_80+), and similarly __hfma2 for float16 in the GEMV operation. For the LOP3 bitwise operations, the compiler reliably emits a single LOP3 instruction from plain C expressions like (w & mask) | magic, so I can write straightforward code and verify the SASS if needed. I need to be careful with CUDA operator macros that torch might define — I'll use the explicit intrinsics like __hsub2 and __hfma2 directly. For the implementation, I'm converting the reinterpreted uint32 result from LOP3 back into half2 or bfloat162 using pointer casting, then extracting individual floats with __low2float and __high2float. Now I'm planning the edits to the CUDA kernel: I'll extend the quantized weight structure to include a pointer to the repacked tensor, have Python append the repacked data right after the original weights for each quantized weight block, and update the parameter builder to consume the extra tensors. The fill functions will produce both fp32 and half2 accumulators while dropping the global sum, using shared memory for the half2 array as uint32 reinterpretation. The gemv_i4 kernel gets a v2 implementation, followed by updates to mla_combine to fill the half2 from context before calling the kernel. I'm also restructuring kda_state to use float4 with 16-row slices, updating mla_attn_chunk with bfloat162 fused multiply-add and prefetching with fp32 flush every 16 iterations, rounding the dense rows output to bfloat16, and letting Python dynamically choose chunk sizes of 32 or 64. Now I'm handling the repacking of quantized weights from a 2D uint8 tensor into a 4D view that the kernel can efficiently read as uint32 values, verifying the byte ordering matches my extraction logic. For the multi-expert case with 64 experts, I'm reshaping and permuting to maintain the same memory footprint while organizing data per-expert, then confirming the stride calculations work out identically whether expressed in uint8 or uint32 units—gate/up and down projections both end up at 294912 uint32 elements per expert. Writing the gemv_i4 kernel... Writing kernel logic... Writing kernel computation... Finishing the half-precision multiply-accumulate operation, then looping through weight updates. I'm realizing there's a bug in how the scaling factor gets applied — it needs to be applied per group rather than globally, so I need to restructure the flush logic to accumulate into per-slot fp32 pairs first, then multiply by the scaling factor at the slot boundary. Now I'm setting up the cross-warp reduction phase where each warp writes its accumulated results into shared memory, then syncing before the final atomic additions to the output buffer. I'm also checking column validity to make sure we don't access out-of-bounds memory when the number of columns is smaller than the warp width. For the second kernel, I'm laying out the shared memory as an array of uint4 elements to pack four half2 values per entry, which keeps the total footprint under 8KB. I'm converting the scaled fp32 values from shared memory into fp16 pairs and packing them efficiently, then tracking that the fp16 accumulator can safely handle up to 8 half-precision multiply-add operations before needing to flush. Now I'm checking the headroom on the accumulator — the products stay well under the fp16 limit of 65504, so flushing at register block 3 and at the end keeps everything stable. The real bottleneck is memory bandwidth: with only 4 loads in flight per warp, I'm hitting a ceiling around 1.2MB total and 1.6TB/s, which isn't enough. The fix is to restructure the loop so all 8 register blocks load upfront into a larger register array, which pushes the in-flight data to 4KB per warp and gets me closer to 8TB/s. Register pressure looks manageable — 32 for the preloaded weights, 8 for accumulators, 8 for intermediate values, 4 for scaling factors, 4 for float accumulators, plus a few more — totaling around 70-80 registers, which fits comfortably in the 128-register budget. The strategy is to issue all 32 weight loads upfront in a strided pattern across the warp, then load the thread-local shared memory values per register block during the compute loop, letting the compiler hide the shared memory latency through scheduling. The key insight is that these 32 loads are independent and can be in flight simultaneously across the warp, with slot boundaries acting as drain points. Across 2368 warps with a couple of slot drains per warp, the latency amortizes well. I'm also considering whether to use non-caching loads like `__ldcs` for the weights since they're streamed once, though L2 pollution isn't a major concern here. Now I'm moving forward with the C2 rewrite, adding prefetch and bf16 half-precision floating-point multiply-add operations. The `kda_state` float4 rework is moderate complexity. Everything else stays as is while I write out the v2 patch and update the CUDA source sections. One more thing to verify: the fill fp32 `t` is still being used by dense_rows (now with bf16 rounding), fill_hh writes to t then th2 from t, conv reads qkvg directly from shared memory, and gemv_i4_T uses a separate smm.vin. For mla_combine, I'm replacing the context-to-th2 logic by filling th2 directly from context (512 values compressed to 64 uint4s) then running gemv_i4. Dropping fill_gsum entirely and removing gsum from SmemG. The register-pressure guard with `__launch_bounds__(256, 2)` stays in place to watch for ptxas spilling. Looking at the performance metrics across these routed-expert stages, there's a stark inconsistency: identical work in L0.B takes 13µs but L1.B jumps to 26µs, suggesting the variance isn't from the computation itself but something about how the stages are being executed or scheduled differently at each level. Clock throttling might be skewing the measurements — I'd need root access to lock GPU clocks, which probably isn't available. Looking at the aggregate timing instead: the full kernel runs in 519µs, but my expected work breakdown (GEMV traffic, barriers, compute stages) only adds up to around 280µs, leaving a significant gap. The MLA stages alone account for 142µs, while the F, G, B, E stages handle roughly 220MB of traffic at an effective 0.9TB/s instead of the expected 3.2TB/s. C1 is puzzling though — it's just a small transposed read operation at 24µs, which seems disproportionately expensive. Now I see the culprit: C1 and C3 are both bottlenecked by serial dependent loads. Each stage runs about 64 iterations of load-then-shuffle-reduce chains where only 1-2 loads can be in flight per warp at a time, giving roughly 64 × 350ns ≈ 22µs of pure latency. The pattern is consistent across the kernel — any loop mixing per-iteration global loads with short compute becomes latency-bound. I need to restructure C1 and C3's transposed GEMV to break this dependency chain. For C2's 94µs bottleneck, the issue is different: with chunk size 32 at L=8232, I'm spreading 258 chunk-tasks across 296 blocks. Each chunk processes 32 rows in 2 passes, but the loads for row l+1 don't issue until after the softmax and reduce of row l finishes — that's a score-reduce chain of shuffles and broadcasts taking ~100 cycles, plus expf and accumulation, totaling 300-400 cycles per iteration. Over 64 iterations (32 rows × 2 passes), that's roughly 200ns × 64 ≈ 12-13µs of latency per chunk. With 258 blocks each handling one chunk, I'd expect ~13µs, but I'm measuring 94µs — 7× worse. The prefetch guards look correct, but I'm wondering if the cache-key-value reads are actually staying L2-hot. The c_kv cache is 8.4MB read once per step from DRAM, and each block reads its 32KB chunk (32 rows × 1KB), which should pipeline after the first microsecond or so. But something's clearly stalling the pipeline. Actually, I think I see the issue now. Each block reads its chunk twice — pass 2 re-reads from L1, which is fine. But the real bottleneck is the critical path per l iteration: I'm prefetching cv4 for l+1 at the top of the loop before computing l, which should overlap the DRAM latency. Except my compute only takes ~200ns while DRAM latency is ~700ns, so I'm stalling ~500ns per l waiting for the next row's data. A pipeline depth of 1 isn't enough — I'd need depth ~4, but that requires keeping 4 rows ahead in registers, which is too many. The alternative is processing multiple rows per iteration as a pair, loading 2 rows ahead instead. A better approach is staging the entire chunk into shared memory first. All 256 threads cooperatively load the 32 rows (32KB total) in one coalesced burst — each thread loads 8 uint4s independently, taking about 1µs. Then I compute entirely from shared memory, which is fast with no stalls. Per block that's roughly 1µs for the load plus 64 rows × ~150ns compute, but the score-reduce shuffle chain is still serial at ~100+ cycles per l per pass, so 32 l × 2 passes × 300 cycles ≈ 19K cycles ≈ 10µs total. With 258 blocks running in parallel across 129 SMs (2 blocks per SM), the bottleneck is that per-block time of ~10-12µs. I could halve the effective latency by processing 2 l's per iteration with interleaved independent chains, or restructure the score computation entirely — have each thread compute the full 512-dot product for one l, so 32 threads handle 32 l's, with each thread reading its l's row from shared memory sequentially. The tradeoff is that while this eliminates the shuffle reduce for scores, the accumulation step (summing p×c_kv across l for each dimension d) now requires cross-thread reduction per dimension, which is worse. So I'm sticking with the lane-16 layout plus shared memory staging and maybe a 2-row interleave to manage bank conflicts. Now I'm reconsidering the F/G/B stages — each is ~20-26µs, and I need to account for the fill time (~2-3µs) plus the per-block GEMV cost with 73KB of weights. The load pattern is roughly 18 slots per task, with each warp handling 2-3 slots and 8 uint4-loads in flight (~4KB per warp). The expected latency should be around 2µs for load overlap plus 2.5µs fill plus 1µs topk plus barrier overhead, totaling ~6µs, but I'm measuring 20-26µs — a 3-4× gap that's frustrating. Comparing to the standalone kernel which was ~14.6µs for 512 tasks (37.7MB), that works out to ~7-8µs per task-wave, yet the megakernel F stage with 256 tasks in one wave is hitting 20+µs, which is actually worse per-wave than the standalone. The standalone had the advantage of preloading th2 and skipping topk entirely. The fill and topk overhead together account for 3-4µs, bringing the gap down to ~11µs versus the standalone's 7µs. The remaining difference likely stems from general megakernel overhead — L2 cache hit rates are probably suffering, and instruction cache misses are a major culprit. The megakernel is enormous with all stages unrolled, potentially 10-20K SASS instructions total, which could balloon to 320KB of instruction footprint. Since each SM executes all stages sequentially, every stage transition causes instruction cache misses as the code streams into the 32-128KB i-cache. I'm thinking about reducing unrolling with pragmas to keep loops rolled where they're large, and I'm noticing that gemv_i4 is being inlined at ~15 call sites, creating 9K instructions of duplicated code. Marking it as __noinline__ would keep just one copy in memory and trade tiny calling convention overhead for massive instruction cache savings. My plan is to start with __noinline__ on gemv_i4, then tackle the C1/C3 transposed gemv with batch prefetch and C2 with shared memory staging. For C2 specifically, I could load chunk rows into shared memory once and have both passes read from it, making the second pass free, plus interleave multiple l iterations to hide latency and potentially restructure the per-thread work around the shared memory data layout. Now I'm working through the implementation details: marking gemv_i4 and maybe moe_topk and the fill functions as __noinline__, then for C2 staging the chunk into shared memory—checking that a 32-element chunk fits in 1KB, so 32KB total for chunk 32, but chunk 64 would need 64KB which exceeds the current budget until I realize the staging can overlay SmemG's unused fields since C2 doesn't use them, keeping everything within the 227KB per-SM limit. With 16K context I'd need 514 chunks at size 32, which exceeds the 264 partial slots available, so I'm bumping maxchunks to 520 to handle this—the memory overhead is acceptable at ~17MB. This means updating both the kernel's shared memory array for C3 combine operations and the Python config to use chunk=32 consistently. For the C1/C3 transposed gemv, I'm reconsidering the kv_b access pattern for q_eff using a repacked w2 layout to optimize row-wise output across the 512×128 matrix. Now I'm working through the warp-level computation where each thread accumulates results across 8 different output rows per u32, with lanes handling column groups of 4 from the 128 total columns. The key is computing scaled quantized values per row-group variant and threading the accumulation efficiently across the warp. The optimization I'm seeing is that batching the loads across 8 row-blocks reduces the shuffle overhead from 10 per row down to 5 per row, matching the current approach but with better throughput by processing multiple blocks together. For the z-handling with HSUB2, each warp loads its z values based on the group and column, and since each warp processes 64 rows (8 row-blocks), the group assignment stays constant within a warp even though it might span group boundaries. The dequantization precomputes the product of scale and quantization factors per column (snq[c] = s[g,c]×qn[c]), storing this as fp16 pairs per lane's 4 columns, then extracts values from each u32 to apply the full dequant formula. With 8 row blocks per warp, I accumulate the partial results from each block's 4 columns across all 32 lanes, then perform a warp-level reduction to get the final 8 row sums—that's about 40 shuffle operations per block. The scaling gets tricky: pairs p1 and p3 are scaled by 16, so I track them separately in the accumulators and divide them down at the end, then combine all the scaled and unscaled accumulator pairs into the final row sum. I can keep the v1 structure mostly intact but hoist the scale and zero-point calculations per group (since g is fixed per warp across 64 rows), then preload the weight words into registers upfront — this minimal change should give the performance boost without major restructuring. The barrier count is around 26, which becomes significant as other parts get faster. I'm considering merging stages to reduce barriers, but most combinations have true dependencies that require them to stay — like D writing to the buffer with atomics and E reading from it. Another angle: instead of each block computing the fill independently with multiple syncthreads, what if only the first block computes it and publishes to global memory, then others read it? That trades compute plus three syncthreads (~2µs) for a single global read latency (~0.7µs). Or I could restructure to a single-pass fill: read input once, compute sum-of-squares, scale using shared memory, then handle the th2 pass — this keeps most data in shared memory and minimizes global reads and syncthreads overhead. The third optimization also leverages the same pattern of reading accumulated values, with per-head loads that are strided across channels. The key issue is that with channel counts up to 514, the loop over channels becomes a serial dependency chain where each iteration loads from the same memory location—though the addresses are known upfront, allowing the compiler to unroll by 4-8 iterations and expose some instruction-level parallelism. The extraction logic pairs rows (r0, r4) and accumulates products per column, then flushes results per row block, with each pair holding two row-wise accumulators. For each column group within a lane's 4 columns, I multiply the weight pair by the corresponding scaled quantized value, accumulating the dot products for rows r0 and r4 separately. After processing all 4 columns in one uint4 load, I flush the 8 partial results (one per row in the block) and perform a warp-wide reduction across all 32 lanes to combine the 128 columns—this takes about 40 shuffle operations per row block, keeping the total per warp around 900 operations, matching the baseline. reasoning
Looking at the performance metrics across these kernel implementations, the T-gemv rewrite brought C1 down significantly from 24.2 to 8.5 microseconds, which is solid progress. C3 improved more modestly to 17.6 microseconds total. But C2 is still stuck at 113 microseconds even with shared memory staging, which is frustrating. The L0.B measurement jumped unexpectedly to 43.9 microseconds for the first stage alone—likely picking up some warm-up overhead on the initial launch, which is throwing off the baseline compared to the previous 13 microsecond measurement. The regressions in E and other stages suggest the dynamic shared memory allocation of 144KB per SM is squeezing the L1 cache significantly on the B200 (where L1 and shared memory share a 228KB pool). With L1 nearly depleted, all those redundant broadcast reads that were previously cached—like the hbuf fills, topk logits, and second-pass S reads—are now hitting L2 instead, which explains the widespread slowdown across stage B and the compute pipeline. The C2 compute loop itself seems fundamentally bottlenecked; with 129 blocks processing 64-element chunks at 8K, each block is doing a 64KB stage load followed by two passes over 64 rows, and the per-row work involves multiple LDS operations that are likely suffering from bank conflicts in the shared memory access pattern. The 8-way bank conflicts mean each LDS.128 operation takes roughly 8 cycles, so across 4 loads per row and 8 warps in the block, I'm looking at significant shared memory pressure — around 32 slow cycles per row per warp. The fix is to pad the row dimension from 64 to 68 uint4 elements to break the conflict pattern. Wait, but padding the rows doesn't actually solve this — the conflict is within a single row's access pattern. Each lane reads offsets 0, 64, 128, ..., 960 within the row, which mod 128 gives only 2 groups, creating an 8-way conflict regardless of row padding. The real issue is the intra-row stride of 64B. I need to either swizzle how the row is stored in memory or change the read pattern so each thread reads 4 consecutive uint4 elements instead of strided ones. Trying different approaches to fix the bank conflicts — either using an XOR swizzle on the indices to spread lanes across distinct banks, or transposing the data layout in shared memory so reads become column-wise accesses, or padding the quad-groups. Leaning toward interleaving how threads handle dimensions so each lane grabs non-consecutive indices that naturally avoid conflicts. Remapping the qeff and pacc indexing to match the new cv4 layout so that qe4 loads stay warp-coalesced and pacc writes maintain consistent dimension ordering for C3a's context. Krf reads also distribute evenly across shared memory banks without conflicts. Now considering whether to reduce dynamic shared memory by staging only 32 rows at a time instead of the full 64-row chunk to stay within the 72KB SmemC2 limit. Reconsidering the register pressure: storing accumulators for both passes simultaneously would need 64 fp32 registers, which is too many. Instead, I could restructure to loop over passes first, then sub-batches within each pass—this means re-staging data per pass (doubling memory traffic to 16.8MB, adding ~1µs), but the synchronization stays clean since all threads in a pass sync together. Though I'm realizing the chunk size of 32 might be too small anyway; need to reconsider whether 64 works better. Actually, stepping back—the real bottleneck isn't the memory loads at all. Both the register-prefetch version (94µs) and the staging version (113µs) are slow, which points to the compute chain being the culprit. With 129 blocks each processing 64 rows across 2 passes, the per-row serial dependencies are brutal: shuffle-reduce operations (4 shuffles + 1 broadcast, each ~15-30 cycles) plus exponential and other math stack up to 350-700 cycles per row, translating to 23-45µs per block just for compute. With only 129 out of 296 blocks active, I'm not hiding enough latency. Let me trace through the instruction count more carefully: roughly 60 instructions per row per thread, which scales to 7680 warp-instructions per block across 8 warps. With 2 blocks per SM and 16 warps total, that's 123K instructions. At perfect issue rate (1 IPC across 4 schedulers), I'd expect around 31K cycles or 15.5µs, but I'm measuring 113µs—so something else is serializing the work beyond just instruction throughput. Looking at the per-row latency chain: the dot product conversion from bf16 to fp32 feeds into 4 shuffles that are dependency-chained (~100 cycles), then a broadcast (~25), then the exponential (~20-30 cycles), compare/branch, and finally the 16 HFMA2 operations. That serial path alone is 200-300 cycles per row. Even though the 16 warps can work independently on different rows, they're all processing the same rows sequentially without synchronization between passes, so the warp scheduler can only overlap work across those 16 independent warps—but the fundamental latency chain per row is still blocking forward progress. The shuffle throughput is the real constraint: with 4-6 shuffles per row across 16 warps, that's roughly 80 shuffle instructions per row-wave per SM, and at 4 shuffles per clock the SM can issue, we're looking at about 20 cycles just for the shuffle stage across 128 rows—around 1.3 microseconds total. The exponential function (SFU/MUFU) adds another 2 operations per row, but the shuffle bottleneck is the tighter limit here. But 1.3 microseconds doesn't explain the 113 microsecond gap. reasoning
So the B200's half-precision math runs at half the throughput of fp32—17.5T versus 34.4T instructions per second—which means packing two values into HFMA2 doesn't actually gain anything per value since you're processing at half speed anyway. My dequantization pipeline using HSUB2 and HFMA2 is hitting that same half-rate bottleneck, which explains why the performance isn't scaling as expected. The key insight is that fp32 FFMA is the fastest scalar path on datacenter Blackwell, so I should redesign the math around fp32 operations with minimal integer overhead. For the GEMV inner loop, I'm extracting nibbles from each u32 (representing 8 values) using a magic fp32 approach: shift and extract each nibble, fuse the AND and OR operations into a single LOP3 instruction to construct the float representation, then subtract the zero offset exactly (since the offset fits within fp32's integer precision up to 2^23+15). The goal is to minimize per-value operations—mostly shifts shared across nibbles, one LOP3 per value, and one FSUB—then fuse that subtraction directly into the FMA with the accumulation. Optimizing the byte extraction with PRMT instructions to reduce shift operations... So if the integer operations run on a separate 64-wide pipeline, I can fit 16 int ops per u32 value, which gives me an integer throughput ceiling of about 9.4 trillion values per second or 4.7TB/s for int4 data. The floating-point side stays well under the 34TB/s limit at around 22TB/s, so the magic-fp32 approach maxes out around 4.5-5TB/s — right at the memory bandwidth ceiling, which is promising. But I need to check how the v1 implementation actually measured against this. The issue with v1 was architectural: it used scalar 32-bit loads instead of vectorized loads, had inefficient shared memory broadcasts across 2 rows, and other bottlenecks. Let me redesign the inner loop using uint4 loads with fp32 magic dequantization, zero-offset presubtraction, and t values loaded per row from shared memory. For each row block of 8 rows, I load t as two float4 vectors shared across 4 columns, then per thread per slot I'm doing 8 uint4 loads plus 2 LDS operations per row block and about 31 operations across 4 columns — roughly 1040 operations per slot plus another 30 for scale and zero handling. That's about double the instruction count compared to the current fp16 version, which uses around 416 half-precision instructions. The new fp32 approach puts most ops on the full-rate fp32 pipe with 16 integer ops running in parallel on the int pipe, giving dual-issue overlap and roughly 16-20 cycles per u32 versus the current 13 half-instructions at half-rate (about 26 cycles total) — so maybe 25% faster and it removes the HSUB dependency chain. Not huge gains though. I'm thinking bigger: what if I process with fp32 but extract only 4 nibbles per u32 by using both nibbles in a single operation? The trick is encoding byte pairs as a single fp32 value where v_pair = 16×v_hi + v_lo, then precomputing contributions per register block. Instead of splitting the weight stream the usual way, I could repack it so a u32 holds 4 low nibbles and 4 high nibbles together, which lets me compute Σ t_even×v_lo + t_odd×v_hi more efficiently without needing to split the byte encoding. But there's a better approach: use dp4a (the int8 dot product instruction on the integer pipeline). I'd quantize the t coefficients per 128-group to int8 with a scale factor, then compute the dot product exactly as an int32 via dp4a on packed u32 values containing 4 int8 elements each. Then I apply the zero-point correction using integer arithmetic: subtracting z times the precomputed sum of quantized values per group, which costs just a couple of integer multiply-add operations per group per column, before scaling by the combined scale factor with a single floating-point multiply-accumulate. The throughput analysis shows this is completely memory-bound — even with the minimal integer operations, I'm hitting around 5.5–5.9 TB/s, well below what the compute pipeline could theoretically deliver. For accuracy, quantizing activations to int8 per 128-value group gives roughly 0.5–1.5% relative error depending on the max-to-RMS ratio within each group, which is comparable to the fp16 baseline, though slightly higher. With W4A8 quantization (4-bit weights, 8-bit activations), the output error from accumulating quantized weight-activation products stays around 0.3% relative error. Propagating this through the network, the activation quantization noise compounds to maybe 1–2% error in hidden layers, which combined with existing precision loss keeps cosine similarity around 0.990 — tight but probably workable. To tighten the margin, I could use finer per-64-element scaling instead of per-128 to reduce quantization error, or rely on int8 dot products for the bulk computation while being selective about where I keep higher precision. The dp4a accumulation stays well within int32 bounds at 244K, and since the reference itself uses bf16 (which has only 8-bit mantissa precision), my int8 quantization error with per-64 scaling is actually comparable to the reference's own rounding — maybe 2× the bf16 rounding error, which is acceptable. For the actual computation, I'm handling the signedness carefully: the v nibbles are stored as unsigned bytes 0–15 (which are positive when interpreted as signed), and t_q ranges from −127 to 127, so the products fit cleanly. I'm packing the rows into u32 words where even rows go into the low nibbles and odd rows into the high nibbles, then unpacking them back out for the dp4a operation. I'm precomputing the quantization values into shared memory by organizing them into even and odd groups per row block, storing them as packed u32 pairs in a compact array. For the correction term, I need the sum of quantization values over each 64-row half-group, which I'll precompute once during the fill phase and store in a small array indexed by half-group. Now for the actual computation: I'm computing t as fp32, then per half-group I find the max absolute value to set the quantization scale, quantizing t to int8 by dividing by that scale. At the slot end, I dequantize using the precomputed scale and correction sum—the dequantization involves an integer multiply-add to compute the corrected dot product, then a floating-point multiply-add to scale by the column scale and quantization scale. I can precompute the product of column scale and quantization scale per slot to reduce this to about 3 operations per column per slot. For the inner loop, I'm loading uint4 values (packing two int4 values per byte) and unpacking them with bit operations—masking the low and high nibbles, then using dp4a instructions to compute dot products with the even and odd quantized values. This gives me about 0.66 operations per value across the four columns. Putting it together: per slot per thread I'm doing 8 global loads, 8 LDS loads, and roughly 170 integer operations plus 12 floating-point operations at the tail—far fewer than before. The issue estimate shows this should hit around 760 cycles per 64KB, or about 0.38 microseconds, which translates to 168GB/s per SM and a ceiling of 25TB/s—I'm load-bound at 5.5–6TB/s as expected. I should verify that dp4a throughput on the B200 is sufficient, since the intrinsic compiles everywhere but I want to confirm the actual rate on that architecture. For C2, I'm considering whether to apply the same dp4a approach to the bf16 cache values, though that requires quantizing per-row at load time and adds complexity. The real issue with C2 is different—it's stalls rather than throughput—so I'm thinking I'll first fix GEMV with dp4a and measure, then come back to C2. One option is to switch C2's dot product from half-rate HFMA2 to full-rate FFMA, which should be a wash in terms of issue slots. Alternatively, I could convert C2's dot to dp4a as well by quantizing the qeff values to int8 per head and extracting them from the bf16 cache on the fly, though that conversion overhead might not be worth it. For now, I'm leaning toward using FFMA with bitshift conversions to extract bf16 components and then doing the dot product and accumulation in full-rate floating-point. The real bottleneck in C2 isn't obvious from the math—the register pressure and stall patterns need actual measurement. On the memory side, I'm also reconsidering the shared memory carve-out: with dp4a, the total smem footprint is around 25KB for GEMV plus 72KB for C2, which dominates and triggers L1 carveout regressions. I'm planning to shrink the C2 staging down to 32 rows with sub-batches, which should cut it to around 36KB and bring the total smem to roughly 37KB per SM—that's 74KB carve-out, leaving L1 at about 150KB. Before I commit to that change, though, I need to verify the L1-carveout theory by testing what happens when I reduce C2 staging and measure the actual driver behavior. Looking at the performance metrics for Stage B, the fill operation averages 3.25µs—higher than Stage F's 2.0µs, possibly due to barrier-release skew—while the gemv operation shows concerning variance with a mean of 3.9µs but a max of 14.8µs for a single task. The total stage time averages 7.1µs with a maximum of 18.6µs, which aligns with the observed wall time of ~21µs when accounting for the barrier overhead. The massive gemv variance across blocks likely stems from DRAM contention: all 296 blocks (73KB each, ~19MB total) hit memory simultaneously at stage start, creating a service queue where tail blocks experience significantly delayed load completion. This explains why the measured max gemv of 14.8µs translates to an effective bandwidth of only ~1.4TB/s—roughly half the standalone gemv performance of 2.6-2.7TB/s. The culprit is the fill operation (smem writes and syncs) executing first in each block, delaying load issuance by 2-3µs before all blocks can issue their memory requests, whereas the standalone kernel had preloaded data ready for immediate staggered load issuance. But wait—the standalone's 2.6TB/s was actually measuring L2-hot bandwidth, not true DRAM performance. The 37.7MB weights fit entirely within the 126.5MB L2 cache after 50 repeated runs, so that number doesn't reflect cold DRAM behavior. The NM=32 run at 3.06-3.2TB/s, which mostly misses L2 (151MB > 126MB), gives a more realistic DRAM-capable rate. The megakernel's 1.3-1.9TB/s still falls short of this 3.2TB/s baseline, even though weights cycle through L2 every step and should be DRAM-cold. The gap might come from pipeline bubbles at stage boundaries—the DRAM pipeline starts empty, and accounting for fill delay (~2-3µs), ramp latency (~1µs), and the time to load 19MB at 3.2TB/s (~5.9µs), plus tail overhead, I'd expect around 9.5-10µs per stage, but measured max-block-total is 18.6µs, leaving roughly a 2× discrepancy. Looking at per-block gemv work now—18 slots distributed across 8 warps means each warp gets 2-3 sequential slots. Each slot involves loading 8 uint4 values, then waiting for the full DRAM latency before computing. Under normal conditions that's about 1-1.3µs per round, so three rounds per warp would be ~4.2µs, but under heavy memory queuing the latency balloons—if 19MB of requests are already queued ahead, latency could hit 4µs or more. The real bottleneck is the serialization: each warp's three dependent rounds force the in-flight demand to ramp up in waves, and the last slot's loads can only issue after earlier slots finish, which multiplies the effective tail and pushes the wall time higher. The fix is to issue all of a block's loads as early as possible rather than sequentially, but that would require too many registers if we tried to load all slots upfront. Instead, I should reduce slots per warp to 1 and increase K-splits to keep warps busy—splitting 18 groups into 4 K-splits gives 8-10 slots across warps, which means roughly one slot per warp and avoids idle warps. That scales to 512 total tasks across 4 matrices, 32 tiles, and 4 splits. With more tasks per block (1.73 tasks/block over 296 blocks), the fill overhead amortizes better since one fill covers two tasks using the same input. Each warp now processes two sequential rounds instead of three because the second task's loads can issue right after the first task's compute phase rather than waiting through three chained slots. The improvement is modest though—the real solution would be to software-prefetch across slots. Looking at the emv_i4 approach with prologue and loop stages, I'm calculating the memory footprint: issuing slot 0's loads in the prologue, then in the loop issuing slot i+1's loads (which requires 32 extra registers for w_next) while computing slot i. With 2368 warps and 16 uint4 values per warp in flight, that's roughly 19MB total—way over the practical limit and maxing out memory-level parallelism. The latency hides behind fill time plus traffic bandwidth, but the register pressure is the blocker: current usage is already 124 out of 128 registers, and double-buffering w adds another 32 registers, causing a spill. I'm exploring alternatives: launching with higher register limits and fewer blocks per SM trades off resident block count and doesn't improve warp count, so latency hiding gets worse. Another option is preloading only 4 register blocks and pipelining the remaining halves within each slot—keeping in-flight memory at 4×512B but issuing the next half during compute of the first, though this doesn't solve the cross-slot latency issue. I'm also considering using CP.ASYNC for the gemv weights on a per-warp basis. CP.ASYNC could free up registers by staging slot weights into shared memory—4KB per warp-slot across 8 warps with 2 buffers gives 64KB, which combined with the existing 25KB SmemG budget totals around 89KB. That fits within the 83KB union limit, and across 2 blocks stays under the 178KB threshold. Each warp would issue 8 CP.ASYNC.16 operations per lane to stage its 4KB slot, then pipeline by committing slot 0 before starting slot 1. Now I'm working through the memory layout for the compute phase—the warp reads from its own 4KB buffer in shared memory, but the current layout causes 8-way bank conflicts when lanes try to load their 4 columns. I need to restructure the staging so CP.ASYNC writes each lane's 16B contiguously in shared memory rather than following the global tile layout. The key insight is to use lane-major addressing: each lane's 8 CP.ASYNC operations write their 8 rows of 16B data to contiguous addresses in shared memory, so when compute reads, each lane gets 128B conflict-free. Meanwhile, the global source for each lane's CP.ASYNC is already 16B contiguous and coalesces properly across lanes per row. I'm setting up a gemv_i4_async kernel with a 2 or 3-stage pipeline using shared memory buffers. Writing the async pipeline logic... cp.async is definitely available on sm_100 since the earlier test kernel compiled and ran successfully with good throughput. The per-warp pipeline approach should be much faster than the block-wide synchronization design I tried before. For the destination addressing, each lane's cp.async operations target consecutive uint4 blocks, so the indexing works out to pack 128B per lane efficiently. Now I'm verifying the global read coalescing: across the 32 lanes, each instruction covers all lanes' reads at the same rb index, which gives me 512B of contiguous source addresses that coalesce properly. I need to implement this in gemv_i4 and also figure out the slot-to-warp mapping since I could have up to 18 slots with at most 3 per warp, which affects pipeline depth. For the pipeline, a 2-deep buffer should be enough to hide one latency cycle: issue two slots, wait on the first, compute it, then issue the third and continue. The in-flight memory footprint with 2 buffers across 8 warps at 4KB each stays within the 64KB shared memory budget. I'm using cp.async.wait_group to synchronize—it's warp-uniform and waits until at most N groups are pending, which fits my needs. The tricky part is handling the edge case where ncols is less than 128: inactive lanes would read out of bounds with cp.async, so I need to either skip the async copy for those lanes and zero the shared memory manually, or use cp.async's source-size variant to guard against OOB reads. Defining the shared memory struct and the cp.async helper function... Writing CUDA kernel code... Writing the main loop... At iteration k, I've committed 2 + k groups total, and wait_group(1) ensures at most 1 group is pending, which means groups 0 through k are guaranteed complete—exactly what I need for the buffer. After computing and issuing the next group into the same buffer with a commit, the invariant holds at 2 + k + 1 committed groups. Now I'm working through the tail iterations where there's no new work to issue. For the compute phase, I'm reading each lane's data from shared memory (8 uint4 values per lane, conflict-free via LDS.128 loads) either into registers or computing directly from shared memory to hide the ~30-cycle LDS latency under the dp4a chain. After wait_group completes, I need to verify that __syncwarp() properly synchronizes the warp's visibility of the cp.async completion. Actually, since each lane issues its own cp.async and reads only its own staged data (lane-major layout), there's no cross-lane shared memory dependency — so __syncwarp() isn't needed at all. For the register savings, dropping the w[8] buffer saves 32 registers, which is good. The real problem is that scales/zeros arrays are small (0.6MB per layer) but DRAM-cold — they're streamed once per step like weights, so their 600–900ns latency per slot stays exposed. I need to prefetch them into a register queue at the time I issue slot k+2's cp.async, using a 2-deep buffer that matches the pipeline: when slot j issues into buffer b, load both z and s uint2s into zq[b], and their latency hides until first use at compute time. So the register footprint is 2 buffers × 2 uint2s × 2 = 8 regs total. The prologue issues slots w and w+8 (cp.async plus z/s loads), then the loop runs. Restructuring the task loop: prologue issues cp.asyncs for this task's slots (only needs addresses), then fill runs if needed (its global loads overlap with the cp.asyncs in flight), then compute executes. This hides both the fill latency and the first weight latency together. Now I'm working through the operation order in gemv_i4 v4: compute the warp's slot list and issue cp.async for the slots, then conditionally run the fill if requested. The key insight is that all warps execute the same sequence uniformly, so the syncthreads is safe. After the fill completes, I can proceed to the compute loop which needs the loaded data, followed by reduction with another syncthreads. The cp.async operations in flight during the fill's syncthreads don't cause issues since they're independent. The remaining error comes from the difference between how we represent the activations versus how the reference rounds to bf16—and that gap is essentially irreducible unless I mimic the reference's bf16 rounding behavior first. So the fix is to round activations to bf16 before quantizing to int16, which brings the net error down to match the reference's bf16 rounding exactly. This means the GEMV dot product stays within ~1e-4 relative error, the weights are exact integers, and the dequantization algebra stays in fp32 with an int-exact core. reasoning
Looking at the reference model's top-8 predictions and comparing them to what we picked, there's a subtle difference in the logit values for positions 8 and 9 that's only one unit of precision apart in bfloat16 format. At layer 3, the selected experts match the reference set exactly, just in a different order, so that's consistent. The remaining discrepancy at layer 2 seems to stem from upstream precision issues—I'm checking whether it's from attention operations, the KDA state updates, or the output projection input, and so far the attention and state math look accounted for. The key issue appears to be in how the residual is accumulated: the reference rounds the output projection to bf16 before adding it to the hidden state (also in bf16), but our implementation accumulates everything in fp32 without those intermediate roundings. To match the reference behavior, I'd need to apply bf16 rounding after each attention and MoE contribution to the hidden buffer. The challenge is that multiple blocks write to this buffer via atomic operations, so I can't round the accumulated sum without serializing. One approach would be to round the buffer values when they're read by the next stage—the E/F blocks could apply bf16 rounding as they load values from the hidden buffer before computing the layer norm, which would capture the rounding effect at the right point in the pipeline. For the MoE output, the atomic accumulation in fp32 introduces nondeterminism around 1e-6, but that's acceptable since the read-time bf16 rounding I'm adding handles the precision gap—the double-rounding effect keeps us within ±1 ULP of the reference. Adding this cheap rounding pass should significantly reduce L2-type flips by eliminating most error cases that land near ULP boundaries, though some residual flip probability will remain. The tolerance of 0.98 holds even with simultaneous flips, and I'm verifying the dense_rows pipeline is now consistent end-to-end: rmsnorm uses bf16-rounded values for the inverse calculation, the norm weight multiplication happens in fp32 then casts to bf16, and fill_quant pre-rounds t to bf16 before the GEMV path—this matches the reference exactly.

reasoning
The seed values are oscillating at the precision boundary—seed 0 flipped back to 0.9946 while seed 2 stayed at 0.9925, and the rounding change just shifted which way the tie breaks rather than improving things systematically. I'm digging into why our implementation still diverges by ±1 ULP at those logits, suspecting the rounding behavior in the residual addition is the culprit. The reference implementation rounds the attention contribution to bf16 before adding it to x, but our approach accumulates attention as fp32 using atomics, then rounds only at read time—that's two rounding operations versus one, causing about 25% of elements to differ by 1 ULP. To match exactly, I need to round the attention output to bf16 before the add, but since o_proj gets assembled from 8 K-split atomic partials, I can't round until the complete sum is ready. This means restructuring the pipeline: writing o_proj to a separate buffer as fp32 atomics, then adding a gated task that rounds just the attention portion and combines it with x. That adds complexity to the DEFG stage—D tasks feed into a counter, then a new 18-task set handles the rounded add operation gated on that counter reaching 144, with E gates tracking the add-task completion. For MoE output, the reference does h + moe_out.to(bf16), but our G writes directly into hbuf with atomics, so I need the same separate-buffer-and-round-add treatment. The next layer's B fill would then compute the outer rounding at read time, but the inner MoE rounding requires that dedicated pass. The tricky part is that hbuf needs to stay as the "x" value for subsequent stages while also being updated by these round-add passes—so I'm restructuring hidden state management so hbuf gets updated only through dedicated round-add operations. I can gate the MoE-add pass within the same DEFG stage, triggered after G completes its writes, then sync before the next B starts. This adds about 18 tasks per layer and one more gate-set, costing roughly 1µs per pass, but it eliminates the ±1ULP hidden-state divergence and keeps logits bit-exact with the reference except for the GEMV d component. The rounding behavior is tricky—logit fp32 values can differ by ~1e-4 relative before bf16 conversion, and near-tie boundaries flip with ~2.5% probability instead of 50%, but bf16 rounding masks most of this. All seeds currently pass with a worst margin of 0.0125, and even two simultaneous flips would only drop to ~0.985, still within bounds. After switching to dp2a, the internal nondeterminism from atomics (~1e-6) almost never crosses bf16 boundaries anymore, so results are now stable across runs—seed 0 consistently gives 0.9946, seed 2 gives 0.9925 repeatedly. +---
+name: b200-kernel-perf-facts
+description: Measured B200 (sm_100) microarch facts that drove the megakernel design
+metadata:
+  type: reference
+---
+
+Measured on the NVIDIA B200 (sm_100, 148 SMs, ~1.97 GHz, HBM3e, 126.5 MB L2) in this workspace:
+
+- HFMA2 (fp16 AND bf16 packed) runs at HALF the fp32 FFMA instruction rate (17.5 vs 34.4 T instr/s at 512 thr/SM). Packed-half math has no throughput advantage over scalar fp32 there; dp4a/dp2a hit 18.5T instr/s (74T MAC/s) and plain LOP3/SHF ~57T. Best scalar dequant-dot path = int8/int16 dp4a/dp2a.
+- 4-byte-per-thread global loads cap ~2.1 TB/s at 512 thr/SM; 16-byte (uint4/LDG.128) loads reach 5.7-6.5 TB/s. Always vectorize 16B.
+- cp.async is effectively dead on sm_100 (emulated LDGSTS): a cp.async-staged GEMV ran SLOWER than register preloads. TMA (cp.async.bulk + mbarrier) is the real async path.
+- __ldcs (evict-first) on streamed weights gave +7% end-to-end by stopping L2 thrash.
… +4 more lines

+---
+name: kimi-megakernel-solution-notes
+description: Final design + gotchas of the Kimi-Linear W4A16 decode megakernel solution (35x)
+metadata:
+  type: project
+---
+
+Final state (2026-07-21): solution.py scores ~35.4x geomean over baseline (0.243/0.250/0.261 ms/tok at ctx 2k/8k/16k, ~4000 tok/s), check.py PASS with worst cosine 0.9924. Hardware was a B200, not the advertised RTX PRO 6000. See [[b200-kernel-perf-facts]].
+
+Design: one cooperative kernel (load_inline, cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel, 256 thr x 2 blocks/SM, dynamic smem ~83KB union), ~13 grid.sync stages; B+KDA-state and D+E+F+G are merged super-stages using spin-gates on atomic counters with producers enumerated before consumers (deadlock-safe under co-residency).
+
+Key numerics lesson: the 0.98-cosine gate is dominated by DISCRETE router top-8 flips on near-ties (margins ~5e-4). Fix was to mimic the reference's own bf16 roundings exactly: bf16-round activations before quantization, bf16-round router/beta logits (reference uses bf16 nn.Linear), bf16-round conv outputs/gate pre-activations, bf16-round the fp32 hidden accumulator at rmsnorm read time. Activations quantized to int16 per 64-group (dp2a dot with int4 weights as bytes) => GEMV error ~1e-4, well under the reference's own bf16 noise.
+

reasoning
Now I'm pulling together the final summary with the key performance metrics—we achieved a 35.4× geomean speedup across different sequence lengths with throughput around 3800-4100 tokens per second, validation checks passed with excellent cosine similarity scores, and the implementation uses a single fused cooperative kernel per step for maximum efficiency.
assistant

Done. solution.py is final: check.py PASS (stable across repeated runs, worst cosine 0.9924 vs the 0.98 bar) and benchmark.py RESULT: OK with a 35.4× geomean speedup over baseline.py (34.0× / 35.4× / 36.8× at ctx 2048/8192/16384 — 0.243/0.250/0.261 ms/tok, ~4,000 tok/s).

What was built. One cooperative CUDA kernel (via load_inline, launched once per step() with cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel) runs the entire 4-layer token step: all int4 dequant-GEMVs, the KDA short conv + gated delta-rule state update, the absorbed MLA latent attention, MoE routing/experts, every RMSNorm and residual, plus the latent-cache append (and first-step cache import) — all inside the same launch, with grid.sync() between phases. Two phase groups are merged further using spin-gates on atomic completion counters (producers enumerated before consumers, safe since all blocks are co-resident), so a full step needs only ~13 grid barriers.

The fused GEMV never materializes bf16 weights: weights are repacked once at load so one u32 holds 8 rows of a column, activations are RMS-normed in shared memory, bf16-rounded (matching the reference's activation dtype), then quantized to int16 per 64-element group — the inner loop is two masks + four dp2a per 32 weights, with the asymmetric zero-point folded in exactly as an integer correction. MLA attention runs FlashMLA-style on tensor cores (mma.m16n8k16 split-KV over 32-row chunks).

A few findings that shaped the result, since the hardware turned out to be a B200 rather than the advertised RTX PRO 6000: packed fp16/bf16 FMA runs at half the fp32 rate on this chip (which killed the classic AWQ half2-LOP3 dequant and motivated the integer-dot path), 4-byte loads cap at ~2 TB/s while 16-byte loads reach ~6, cp.async is effectively emulated and slower than register staging, and evict-first (__ldcs) loads on the weight stream bought ~7% by stopping L2 thrash. The last correctness hazard was discrete top-8 router flips on ~5e-4 near-ties; mimicking every bf16 rounding the reference itself performs (router/beta logits, conv outputs, the bf16 hidden state) made those resolve the same way as torch and stabilized the margin.

Remaining headroom (documented in the code and my notes): the weight stream still runs at ~1.5–2 TB/s wall against a ~6 TB/s ceiling — closing that would need TMA bulk pipelines or warp-specialized producer/consumer staging, since the 128-register budget rules out deeper register double-buffering.

Two phase groups are merged further using spin-gates on atomic completion counters (producers enumerated before consumers, safe since all blocks are co-resident), so a full step needs only ~13 grid barriers. **The fused GEMV** never materializes bf16 weights: weights are repacked once at load so one u32 holds 8 rows of a column, activations are RMS-normed in shared memory, bf16-rounded (matching the reference's activation dtype), then quantized to int16 per 64-element group — the inner loop is two masks + four `dp2a` per 32 weights, with the asymmetric zero-point folded in exactly as an integer correction. MLA attention runs FlashMLA-style on tensor cores (`mma.m16n8k16` split-KV over 32-row chunks). A few findings that shaped the result, since the hardware turned out to be a B200 rather than the advertised RTX PRO 6000: packed fp16/bf16 FMA runs at *half* the fp32 rate on this chip (which killed the classic AWQ half2-LOP3 dequant and motivated the integer-dot path), 4-byte loads cap at ~2 TB/s while 16-byte loads reach ~6, `cp.async` is effectively emulated and slower than register staging, and evict-first (`__ldcs`) loads on the weight stream bought ~7% by stopping L2 thrash. The last correctness hazard was discrete top-8 router flips on ~5e-4 near-ties; mimicking every bf16 rounding the reference itself performs (router/beta logits, conv outputs, the bf16 hidden state) made those resolve the same way as torch and stabilized the margin. Remaining headroom (documented in the code and my notes): the weight stream still runs at ~1.5–2 TB/s wall against a ~6 TB/s ceiling — closing that would need TMA bulk pipelines or warp-specialized producer/consumer staging, since the 128-register budget rules out deeper register double-buffering.
